Preface 
 
The Master thesis was conducted by Luise Dennin in the period from 4th September 2024 to 
24th February 2025 at the Department of Civil and Mechanical Engineering at the Technical 
University of Denmark. Associate professor Philip Skov Halding is the supervisor for the thesis 
that constitutes 30 ECTS points and leads to the culmination of the Master of Science in 
Architectural Engineering. It is assumed that the reader has a background knowledge equivalent to 
a Master student of Civil Engineering.

6 
Abstract 
 
Hollow Core Slabs (HCS) are probably among the most used building components since the 
industrialization of construction in the early 1960s. The scale and potential of their structural reuse 
are significant in terms of waste and CO2 reduction. With the legal requirements for the gradual 
reduction of CO2 footprints of future Danish buildings, architects and engineers will face an 
ongoing challenge in finding effective ways to comply.  
 
This study explores the technical feasibility of repurposing reclaimed concrete hollow core slabs as 
vertical load-bearing elements, expanding their reuse potential in new construction. A literature 
review identified general challenges in structural concrete reuse, while an experimental proof of 
concept was conducted on reclaimed hollow core slabs from a deconstructed industrial building in 
Aarhus. The slabs were longitudinally cut and subjected to axial loading at three different 
eccentricities (0, 20, and 55 mm) to assess failure mechanisms and load-bearing capacity. 
 
Results revealed that longitudinal cracking, influenced by the hollow core geometry, played a key 
role in the failure, with splitting and concrete crushing affecting load redistribution. Load 
eccentricities up to 20 mm did not significantly impact the ultimate load, whereas greater 
eccentricity (55 mm) led to earlier failure due to combined compression and bending effects. An 
analytical assessment showed that material strength is a decisive factor in determining the remaining 
load-bearing capacity, necessitating thorough testing beyond archival data. The application of 
knowledge level factors comparable to Eurocode 8-III was found suitable for conservative peak load 
estimations, while initial cracking loads can be effectively predicted according to the theory of 
Schlaich and Schäfer. 
 
A modular structural system, ReHoCS, was proposed to integrate reclaimed hollow core slabs as 
both walls and slabs in building designs, ensuring future adaptability and design for disassembly. 
The study emphasizes the importance of uniform load distribution, adequate lateral restraint, as 
well as considerations for fire safety and sound insulation in future applications. These findings 
support the broader implementation of structural concrete reuse, enhancing both environmental 
and financial feasibility in the construction industry. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Keywords: Structural reuse, circular construction, modularity, concrete hollow core walls, precast concrete 
 
 
==PAGE:7==
 
7 
 
 
 
 
Hollow core slab (HCS) 
 
A standardized, load-bearing building component 
usually used for floors. It was introduced in the 
1960s, and has a width of 1.2 or 2.4 m, span lengths 
ranging from 4.0 to 12.0 m and common heights of 
180, 220, 270, 320 or 400 mm. Made from (high 
strength) concrete, the slabs were reinforced with 
slack reinforcement in the past and since the 1980s 
prestressed with strands at the bottom.

==PAGE:11==
 
i 
Glossary 
 
CIRCULAR ECONOMY 
A closed cycle in which resources are recycled, upcycled and downcycled and where the material 
value is retained as much as possible while using a minimum amount of new resources. 
 
COMPONENT 
Components are individual (concrete) elements, e.g. beams, columns or slabs that make up a load-
bearing structure. 
 
CONCRETE 
Concrete refers to modern concrete with Portland clinker which is usually reinforced with steel 
bars.  
 
DESIGN FOR DISASSEMBLY (DfD) 
Approach to the design of a product that facilitates disassembly at the end of its useful life in a way 
that enables components and parts to be reused, recycled or recovered. 
 
DECONSTRUCTION 
Deconstruction is the process of dismantling structures in a way that enables materials to be 
salvaged. 
 
DISASSEMBLY 
Non-destructive taking-apart of a construction into constituent materials or components. 
 
LOAD-BEARING STRUCTURE 
The part of a construction that carries loads and resists forces. For a building it is most commonly 
an assembly of components: walls, slabs, columns, beams. 
 
MODULARITY 
A concept where the interdependence of independent components in a system is minimized.  
 
RECLAMATION 
The activity of saving useful construction materials or components from waste. 
 
REUSABILITY 
Ability of a material, product, component or system to be used in its original form more than once 
and maintain its value and functional qualities during recovery to accommodate reapplication for 
the same or any purpose. Reuse means using an item for another or the same purpose while keeping 
it intact.  
 
SIMPLICITY 
Quality of an assembly or system that is designed to be straightforward, easy to understand and 
meet performance requirements with the least amount of customization. 
 
STRUCTURAL REUSE 
Structural reuse refers to the reuse of entire load-bearing structures, units or components from a 
donor building in a new building. 
 
UPCYCLING 
Upcycling means converting discarded materials into something of equal or greater value.

1 Introduction 
 
 
 
1 Introduction 
Gordon Matta-Clark, Conical Intersect, Paris 1975 
==PAGE:17==
 
2 
 
Context and Motivation  
Current impact of the construction sector 
The construction sector claims the largest portion of 
Denmark’s environmental footprints – 31% of the 
total material footprint and 17% of the total carbon 
footprint, while also accounting for 40% of the 
waste production (Keys et al., 2023; Ministry of 
Environment of Denmark, 2021). More than two 
million tons of concrete waste are generated 
annually in Denmark while the production of new 
building components leads to substantial CO2 
emissions (Dansk Teknologisk Institut, 2017). 
Consequently, the construction sector emerges as a 
crucial player in the transition toward circular 
economy. The emphasis lies on promoting extensive 
reuse and recycling of building materials and 
components, particularly from the demolition of 
existing structures, thereby diminishing the necessity 
for new materials in new construction projects 
(Assefa & Ambler, 2017). 
 
Potential of reclaiming structural elements 
Obsolete buildings are often structurally sound 
before transformation or demolition, which usually 
occurs for reasons unrelated to material quality or 
structural 
integrity 
(M. 
Pilegaard, 
personal 
communication, 17 October 2023). The load-
bearing elements of these buildings are typically 
well-preserved during their service life as they are 
protected from the weather. Their predominantly 
concrete components could be reused in new 
projects (E. Stenberg, personal communication, 20 
October 2023). Concrete structures often undergo 
low-value recycling, being crushed for road bases or 
backfilling purposes rather than being preserved at 
the element scale and used in a load-bearing context.  
 
Circular approaches to comply with legislation  
The Danish building legislation is restricting the 
environmental impact of the building industry by 
limiting the maximum carbon footprint of new 
buildings, challenging developers, architects and 
engineers to foster the sustainable transformation of 
the sector. Structural reuse will help developers 
comply with building regulations as reused elements 
currently account for 0 kgCO2 in Life Cycle 
Assessments. At the same time, a new law has come 
into place from 1st July 2024 with a transition period 
until 1st July 2025 from when a resource mapping 
prior to the demolition is obligatory for buildings 
with a net room area larger than 250 m2.  
 
 
Context and  
Motivation 
 
Structural reuse as a way to 
reduce waste production, save 
resources and support 
compliance with the building 
legislation. 
 
800.000 m3  
Denmark’s yearly concrete waste 
==PAGE:18==
 
3 
Rationale of the research project 
 
“This building component [HCS] is probably among the most used building components at all 
since the industrialization of construction in the early 1960s. So, the scale and potential of your 
concept is huge in terms of CO2 reductions in circular construction. In relation to the reduction 
roadmap and the recently announced legal requirements for the step-by-step reduction of future 
Danish buildings' CO2 footprint, it will be an ongoing challenge for architects and engineers to find 
means to meet these requirements. We already feel the headache.” 
 
Anders Park, Senior Project Manager at Henning Larsen (Mail from 13.08.2025) 
 
Availability of used concrete hollow core slabs in Denmark 
Hollow core slabs became popular in Europe in the 1960s and 1970s, particularly in multi-story 
residential buildings, commercial buildings, and industrial facilities (Huuhka, 2022). As floor 
systems usually make up for the biggest share of the mass of the load-bearing structure, their reuse 
can support the reduction of carbon emissions in new building projects and avoid the production 
of new concrete (Asam, 2007). DTU researchers created a model with information from the Danish 
Central Register for Buildings and Dwellings (BBR) and found that the amount of demolished square 
meters of residential buildings in Denmark built before 2010 is likely to increase until 2060 up to a 
number of 5 million m2 per year as illustrated in Fig. A 1 (Andersen & Negendahl, 2023). The 
estimation of built-in hollow core slabs in Denmark is more challenging as structural concrete 
elements in general are neither an independent waste category of StatBank Danmark (Statistics 
Denmark) nor are they registered in the Bygnings- og Boligregistret (The Central Register for Buildings and 
Dwellings). As an alternative to the analysis of historical building data, current producers of precast 
concrete elements in Denmark were contacted. Finn Passov, technical manager of the Danish precast 
company Spæncom, estimated that in the past Spæncom has produced between 100.000 and 
200.000 m2 of hollow core slabs per year, with the majority having a thickness of 22 or 27 cm. The 
span lengths of prestressed hollow core slabs, in production since the 1980s, are typically in the range 
of 6 to 10 m, while in the 1960s and 1970s mildly reinforced slabs were fabricated with limited 
spans. According to the sales team, Spæncom currently produces  6.000 m2 of hollow core slabs per 
week (ca. 340.000 m2 per year), a number that has been stable during the last years. Assuming a 
standard width of 1.2 m, this results in approximately 5 km of HCS per week. The available span 
length, slab thickness, the reinforcement layout, and hollow core geometry differ depending on the 
manufacturer and time of production (Fig. 1). 
 
 
 
Fig. 1 
Variety of geometrical layouts of HCS (here from producer CRH Concrete) 
 
 
==PAGE:19==
 
4 
Suitability of precast elements for structural reuse 
Prefabricated elements are ideal for structural reuse because of their standardized nature with 
respect to material properties and geometry. While reusing walls in new buildings is challenging 
due to their limited and often insufficient height as elaborated in chapter 2.1.1, hollow core slabs 
offer greater flexibility, as they can be cut to any size smaller than their original span length.  
Manufacturers of prefabricated concrete elements are further already using labeling systems that 
give comprehensive information about, e.g. the origin of the element, its purpose and properties. 
The technical and financial feasibility of floor slab reuse has already been shown by different 
(research) projects (e.g. Fig. 2 and Fig. 3), while the Norwegian standard NS 3682 already describes 
a testing procedure to ensure the structural soundness of reused hollow core slabs. A Danish 
standard for HCS reuse is currently under development.  
 
 
 
Fig. 2 
Deconstruction and testing of used hollow core slabs for (P)RECAST project by KINGO and DTI 
 
Fig. 3 
Reuse of cast-in place floor slabs as primary (a) or secondary system (b) by (Küpfer et al., 2024) 
Given the limited research on scaling up wall reuse and the lack of studies on the impact of a 
typology change as for repurposing slabs as walls, this thesis project aims to address these gaps. 
The hollow core slab wall (HCSW) concept can be considered upcycling as the latter means 
converting discarded materials into something of equal or greater value (Center of Climate Literacy 
(Climate Lit), 2025). In this case, the value lies in utilizing the component as a load-bearing element.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:20==
 
5 
Objectives and Scope 
The main research goal is to explore the potential for repurposing discarded hollow core slabs, 
commonly used for floors, as vertical structural elements that can resist vertical loads. Both 
architectural and engineering perspectives are considered. Full-scale tests are incorporated as a lack 
of experimental data was identified for reclaimed structural concrete at element scale and vertical 
hollow core panels under axial loading in general.  
 
The central research questions address the structural behaviour of HCSW under axial loading and 
their building integration: 
 
1. What is the axial load-bearing capacity and failure mechanism of used concrete hollow core 
slabs that are repurposed as walls? 
2. Which analytical methods are suitable to estimate the remaining load-bearing capacity of 
hollow core slab walls (HCSW)?  
3. How does the one-sided pre-tensioning of hollow core slabs influence their axial load-
bearing capacity when repurposed as walls? 
4. How can HCSW be integrated into new buildings, and what limitations exist? 
 
Methodology 
Different methods have been used to allow for a holistic investigation of the research questions: 
 
1. Interviews with an engineer and a sustainability expert from ABC Rådgivende Ingeniører were 
carried out, focusing on their experience of structural concrete reuse, to identify current 
barriers and future opportunities to enable the wider structural reuse of HCS.  
2. Site visits of structural reuse projects (Gellerupparken and Thoravej 29) allowed for an 
insight into current practices and challenges related to structural concrete reuse. 
3. A comprehensive literature review identified current efforts of wall reuse and 
possibilities to integrate the elements in building constructions. The feasibility of 
repurposing HCS as walls was studied by studying existing, experimental investigations on 
hollow core panels. 
4. Hand calculations, analytical and numerical methods created a theoretical basis for 
the determination of the structural behaviour of HCS-walls under axial loading, covering 
ultimate loads, failure mechanisms and expected cracking patterns. 
5. Experimental testing of discarded concrete hollow core slabs provided by the Danish 
demolition company P. Olesen was carried out, covering non-destructive and destructive 
material tests as well as full-scale testing to determine the structural capacity under axial 
load with varying eccentricity. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:21==
 
6 
2 State-of-the-Art: Structural concrete reuse 
 
 
2 State-of-the-Art 
    Structural concrete reuse 
Demolition of housing in Cabrini-Green 
==PAGE:22==
 
7 
Several completed building projects across Europe have shown that incorporating reclaimed 
concrete elements can cut the carbon footprint of new constructions by up to 50%(Küpfer & Fivet, 
2023). A comprehensive understanding of the interplay between materials, components, structure 
and the influences of processing during construction and deconstruction is essential to maximize 
the reuse potential.  This chapter reviews the barriers to structural reuse at both the component 
and building structure level and presents current efforts to overcome these challenges. A focus is 
thereby laid on the structural reuse of wall and slab elements. The systemic challenges are briefly 
summarized in the following section to contextualize this thesis project within the broader barriers 
structural reuse currently faces. 
 
Systemic challenges for structural reuse elements 
The systemic challenges to the widespread adoption of structural concrete reuse include financial 
feasibility, a lack of legislation, organizational effort and insufficient knowledge regarding the 
execution of structural reuse projects. Another significant barrier is the absence of a certification 
system that would enhance the reliability of reclaimed structural elements. It would support the 
decision-making process that involves real-estate developers, insurance companies and banks 
(Küpfer & Fivet, 2023; Luise Dennin & Annika Bang Thomsen, 2023). The large-scale replicability 
of structural reuse is crucial for improving the financial viability and avoid numerous costly 
transformations (Belgian Building Research Institute et al., 2021). Overall, more research is 
necessary to facilitate the commercialization of structural reuse (Bastien-Masse et al., 2024). 
 
In order to be considered a viable alternative to conventional precast concrete elements, structural 
reuse elements need to be: 
 
1. Timely available 
2. Easy to reintegrate (Availability of historical documentation, acceptable tolerances, etc.) 
3. Structurally sound (Including structural, fire safety sound insulation aspects) 
4. Financially feasible (Sum of deconstruction, testing, storage and transport) 
 
 
 
Reclaimed hollow core slabs at P. Olesen, Jylland 
==PAGE:23==
 
8 
2.1 
Component level 
 
“Recycling an element in almost its full size is by far the best form of recycling that we can practice.” 
  
Lars Reimer, Technical Manager at CRH Concrete A/S  
(‘Construction resource bank – recycling of hollow core tires’, 2024) 
 
2.1.1 
Challenges 
 
 
 
Fig. 4 
Rendering of structural reuse demonstrator by architecture firm Lendager (Left) and reclaimed concrete column at DTU (Right) 
Dimensional variety and limitations 
(Tomczak et al., 2023) state that contrarily to new elements that can be manufactured to precise 
specifications, reclaimed components come with predefined dimensions and quality limitations. 
Insufficient knowledge regarding the appropriate deconstruction and handling of structural 
concrete elements can restrict their reusability. (Asam, 2007) found that ca. 10% of a reclaimed 
concrete structure intended for reuse is lost due to damage incurred during dismantling, 
transportation, cleaning and cut-offs during in preparation for new use. Additionally, discrepancies 
between the standard dimensions of components used in the past and requirements of new 
buildings are challenging as the wall height or span of slab elements may not be sufficient. The 
potential for reuse is further diminished if the connection areas are damaged during deconstruction 
(See Fig. 4). The reuse toolkit from the Interreg FCRBE (Facilitating the circulation of reclaimed building 
elements) project therefore recommends maintaining a surplus provision of reused elements during 
the supply, as certain elements may prove unsuitable for the installation on-site (Belgian Building 
Research Institute et al., 2021).  
 
Need for appropriate documentation and uncertainty about material quality 
Prof. Lisbeth Ottosen from DTU explained the uncertainties associated with structural reuse: “They 
[the structural elements] may have been outside under different degrees of protection or have been 
exposed to different physical stresses. Therefore, we have to assume that all materials are different. 
This requires documenting the quality.”(Christina Täkker, 2022). Hanna Walter, a sustainability 
consultant at Insentis AS in Oslo, stated in an interview that it is hard to make someone guarantee 
for a product for which proper documentation might initially not be available (Hanna Walter, 
personal communication, 17 September 2024). This is a challenge compounded by the absence of 
a standardized system to guarantee for the element quality over the years following their 
implementation. 
 
 
==PAGE:24==
 
9 
 
Deterioration of material 
According to (Asam, 2007), the weathered surfaces of outer walls can restrict their reuse, as damage 
to the concrete matrix has been frequently observed. Furthermore, it is noted that outer walls with 
core insulation, often consisting of carcinogenic mineral wool, are difficult to reuse due to the high 
costs of separation and the potential risks they pose to the health of construction workers. The 
influence of creep on the reusability of structural concrete elements is yet to be investigated. 
 
Call for standardization of reclaimed elements 
(Estrella et al., 2024) found that limitations in element length pose a challenge for structural reuse. 
They argue that only widespread implementation at an industrial scale can unlock the potential of 
component reuse to reduce carbon emissions and resource consumption of the construction 
sector. Further, they assert that the lack of standardized components designed for deconstruction 
leads to increased costs during the disassembly and reassembly phases, thereby negatively impacting 
the financial viability of structural reuse. The limited stock of a donor building, the unique 
geometries of the different elements, and potential losses during deconstruction and transport 
create uncertainties for planners. The reintegration of unchanged components is desirable since 
any additional processing negatively affects the economic viability. Precast elements have the 
highest reuse potential, particularly floor slabs and interior walls, accounting for up to 42% of the 
building substance in the case of GDR-type apartment buildings (Asam, 2007).  
 
Simplicity and flexibility in the structural articulation as drivers of reuse 
Aiming for simplicity of the element’s structural behaviour supports its reuse potential in the future. 
(Bertin et al., 2019) found that “[…] the more an element is subjected to a simple stress, the less 
specific it will be, which will increase its chances and fields of subsequent reuse. For a complex 
element, it will be even more difficult to find a use configuration similar to its initial use.”. Architect 
Mark Whitby explained that by using neoprene rubber bearings similar to those in bridges, vertical 
elements can act as pure compression elements and are not subjected to a moment as in a rigid, 
fixed concrete structure.(M. Whitby, personal communication, 16 October 2023) 
 
2.1.2 
Structural integrity of reuse elements 
 
Determination of the material strength 
Dansk Standard has been designated by CEN (Comité Européen de Normalisation) to lead the 
development of a common European standard for testing methods of reclaimed structural concrete 
elements. A research project led by DTU’s structural reuse group focuses on the material scale and 
covers massive concrete elements. A Danish standard under the title “Guidelines for reuse assessment 
of structural concrete components” will be published in 2025 and outlines a testing scheme for reclaimed 
concrete elements. However, the testing of HCS is not included in the new standard. In February 
2022, the Norwegian standard NS 3682 “Standard for reuse for hollow core slabs for reuse” was issued. 
The aim of the standard is that “(…) existing hollow core slabs can be documented in a similar way 
as newly produced hollow core slabs.” (Standard Norge, 2022). It contains a guideline on 
deconstruction, transport, testing methods and reintegration in new buildings. It is not intended 
that the slab changes its typology (e.g. from slab to wall). The European standard EN 13791 deals 
with the assessment of in-situ compressive strength in structures and precast concrete components 
and indicates methods to e.g. calculate the characteristic compressive strength of concrete elements 
based on destructive core and cube tests. (Knab & Sodeikat, 2015) stated that the standard is not 
uncontroversial for existing buildings, as it can deliver results that are on the uncertain side.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:25==
 
10 
Structural design with reclaimed structural concrete elements  
In general, reclaimed concrete elements must meet the same structural and aesthetic requirements 
as new elements (Heckmann et al., 2024; Mettke et al., 2014). To facilitate their reintegration in 
new buildings, it is essential to ensure their mechanical performance, fire safety as well as acoustic 
and thermal properties. An uncertainty associated with structural reuse is the remaining service life 
of concrete elements and the impact of degradation processes on their structural performance 
(Suchorzewski et al., 2023). (Asam, 2007) calls for “a generally acknowledged quality system” that 
would include a certification system for structural reuse components. Two reports on the 
mechanical and chemical testing of reclaimed concrete hollow core slabs in Poland and Denmark 
demonstrated that the elements remain in good condition despite their age of 45 and 55 years, 
respectively. How the durability of the elements can be effectively considered in the structural 
design remains unresolved (Ajdukiewicz et al., 2013),(Martins et al., 2023),(AU Engineering, 2025).  
 
An overview of the complex network of technical rules, standards, and regulations relevant to the 
reuse of concrete elements at national and European level can be found in the Appendix (Fig. A 2 
and Fig. A 3). In Denmark, the Danish building regulations (BR18) set the standards for 
construction, including the use of reclaimed materials. They cover structural safety, fire safety, and 
energy efficiency. At present, the regulatory landscape for reusing material is not harmonized across 
the EU. When a construction product is introduced to the European market, it must comply with 
the safety and performance requirements outlined in the Construction Products Regulation (EU 
No 305/2011). This includes providing a Declaration of Performance and obtaining CE marking, 
which indicates compliance with overall EU safety, health, and environmental standards. Due to 
the inhomogeneity of structural concrete elements, CE marking is currently not available. However, 
in 2023, the Committee on Industry, Research and Energy of the European Parliament called for a 
simplified performance declaration process to support large-scale reuse (Committee on Industry, Research 
and Energy & Pascal Arimont, 2023). For now, calculation methods described in the valid Danish 
standards for (precast) concrete can be utilized to prove the structural soundness of concrete 
elements, as it was done for the Danish structural reuse pilot (P)RECAST: 
 
o DS/EN 1168: Hollow core slabs 
o DS/EN 13224: Ribbed floor elements 
o DS/EN 13225: Linear Structural Elements 
o DS/EN 13747: Floor plates for floor systems 
o DS/EN 14992: Wall elements 
 
The Danish building regulations mandate the certification of reclaimed structural elements by a 
checking engineer (Danish: certificeret statiker). Lone Hedegaard Mortensen from ABC Rådgivende 
Ingeniører said in an interview (p. 142), that particularly for the higher consequences classes it is 
challenging to find a checking engineer. With their signature, they are personally liable for the safety 
of the elements. If CE marking existed as a form of quality assurance, the certification process of 
these elements would be easier. 
 
For the project Thoravej 29 in Copenhagen’s neighborhood Nordvest, concrete double T-decks 
were repurposed on-site as ramps (Fig. 5). In conversation with structural engineer Anna-Olivia 
Myrtue from ABC Rådgivende Ingeniører, it was found that the structural capacity was calculated using 
the material strength of the decks indicated in the historical structural calculations (Read the 
interview from p.138). ABC contacted the original manufacturer of the precast elements to obtain 
more detailed information on the material properties. Non-destructive or destructive material tests 
were not carried out. The responsibility for the safety and durability of the elements was finally 
taken by the building developer Bikubenfonden. 
 
==PAGE:26==
 
11 
Arne Killerich from AKi Rådgivning was the responsible engineer for the new headquarters of the 
demolition company Tscherning in Høje Taastrup which incorporates reused HCS and massive 
concrete wall elements (See Fig. 5). A sample of the reinforcement was tested and the concrete 
strength found in the historical documentation reduced by 20% to create a safety margin in the 
calculations of the remaining load-bearing capacity of the structural elements. 
 
 
 
Fig. 5 
Reference projects of structural concrete reuse in Denmark: Tscherninghuset (Left) and Thoravej 29 (Right)

With regard to the reusability of concrete components as structural elements, a grading system 
depending on the visually controlled condition was developed by (Devènes et al., 2023). This 
system evaluates the local extent and global extent of the damage, to determine whether an element 
is suitable for (load-bearing) reuse (Table 2). 
 
Table 2 Grading system for reusability by (Devènes et al., 2023) 
 
 
(Widmer et al., 2023) proposed a structural design scheme for the integration of reclaimed 
structural elements into new buildings (Fig. A 4). The target structure is developed after analyzing 
the source building. First, the floor slabs are allocated based on the available span lengths. Then, 
the vertical elements are incorporated into the design, followed by the check of second order effects 
and the connection design. If necessary, strengthening measures are implemented and finally the 
seismic resistance is evaluated. 
 
(Küpfer & Fivet, 2023) defined criteria to compare projects involving reclaimed concrete pieces, 
aiming to maximize their potential from an environmental but also organizational perspective. They 
cover the extraction method, the reclaimed structural capacity for the new project, which includes 
the use of the material’s compressive strength and/or tensile strength. Additionally, the influence 
of the donor construction method on the reusability is rated. The latter is closely related to the 
ability to preserve existing connections. Another criterion considers the extent of new material 
input and the constraints that may arise for the new design layout.  
 
Fire safety 
For reused concrete structures, fire safety must take into account possible local damage from the 
use as well as the TSP (transport, storage, processing) phases. It is essential to verify whether the 
donor structure was built under less stringent fire safety regulations and if the building typology 
required lower safety standards than those demanded for the future building. Residential 
construction for example is more rigorous with respect to the period of fire resistance compared 
to industrial construction (N.R. Naber, 2012).  
==PAGE:28==
 
13 
This fact was used as a rationale to initiate the implementation of reclaimed structural elements in 
industrial buildings. The fire resistance of structural concrete elements can be enhanced by 
additional (mortar) layers to increase the overall thickness of the element and/or by increasing the 
concrete cover over the prestressing steel. 
 
The Concrete Center recommends using a simplified method for analyzing existing structures and 
determining the fire resistance based on the standard for structural fire design of concrete 
structures (EN 1992-1-2 Part 1-2: General rules - Structural fire design). This standard provides 
the required cross-sectional area for a specified fire resistance period. As a conservative estimate, 
it can be assumed that 70% of the section is used during a fire. To enhance the fire resistance of a 
structural concrete member, finishes can be applied to reduce the temperature of the main 
reinforcement bars. If additional protection is necessary, options such as cementitious sprays, 
intumescent coatings, or board materials can be employed. It is crucial to ensure that the protective 
layer remains intact throughout the duration of the design fire (Tony Jones & Octavian Lalu, 2024). 
 
Note for HCS 
(N.R. Naber, 2012) notes that in the case of hollow core slabs, the web can fail due to shear stresses 
resulting from temperature differences between upper and bottom side which leads to different 
thermal expansions. This factor must be considered in structural fire design. The use of higher 
strength concrete, a common material for HCS, can decrease the fire resistance of building 
structures, as the risk of spalling, the detachment of the concrete surface, increases significantly 
(Lublóy, 2020).  
 
Note for walls 
If walls are exposed to fire, a sharp thermal gradient can develop along the thickness, potentially 
leading to the bowing of the structural element. This results in an additional eccentricity of the 
vertical loads, thereby increasing the risk of buckling. High temperatures generally increase the risk 
of concrete spalling, which can ultimately compromise the integrity of the entire wall (Martins et 
al., 2023). In comparison to conventional wall elements, it has been found that e.g. the Danish 
precast concrete producer Betonelement offers walls with a fire resistance of BS60 for walls with a 
thickness ≥ 120 mm and an approximate cover layer of 25 mm over the reinforcement. They also 
provide a higher class (BS120) for walls with a thickness of 180 mm or greater and an approximate 
cover layer of 35 mm (‘Concrete walls’, 2025). 
 
2.1.3 
Structural wall reuse: Pilot projects 
The Reuse Toolkit distinguishes between two wall types for structural reuse: The reinforced concrete 
(RC) type which must adhere to calculations outlined in Eurocode 2 and, if part of the bracing, has 
to comply with all relevant checks, including those for the joints. If uncertainties about the 
reinforcement remain, the reused element can be enhanced with perimeter reinforcement. The 
second type proposed by the FCRBE project is the masonry type which allows for the use of non-
reinforced elements. In this case, the wall is confined by vertical and horizontal wall ties, creating 
a compression link between wall and the adjacent elements (Belgian Building Research Institute et 
al., 2021). 
 
Recently, the second approach has been demonstrated using a rectangular, reused wall piece within 
a RC perimeter beam by Ramboll in collaboration with DTU Structural Lab and with irregular 
concrete pieces by CRH Concrete (Fig. 6). Søren Malund Thomsen from the demolition company 
KINGO mentioned difficulties finding suitable elements at a demolition site, where predominantly 
150 mm wide elements were available while walls today typically have a thickness of 200 mm. 
 
==PAGE:29==
 
14 
 
 
Fig. 6 
Shear wall (P)REUSE: Reused wall piece with perimeter RC (Left) and CRH wall with reused concrete pieces (Right) 
2.2 Building system level 
 
“I don't think that we can continue designing buildings without the construction engineers also 
looking into the environmental impact from their part.” 
 
Lone Hedegaard Mortensen, Head of Sustainability at ABC Rådgivende Ingeniører 
Interview 20.09.2024 (p. 142) 
 
2.2.1 
Integration of reused concrete walls in building constructions 
Used concrete walls are inflexible regarding door and window openings, as the reinforcement 
layout is specifically designed for these elements and the addition of new openings requires 
considerable labour. Interior walls that were reused by (Asam, 2007), were strengthened with 
transport and ring-anchor reinforcement after deconstruction. Asam further asserted that more 
building designs allowing for selective deconstruction are necessary to increase the overall reuse 
quota. The question of how reclaimed structural concrete elements can be reintegrated in a manner 
that facilitates their future deconstruction was discussed by (Webster & Costello, 2005). They state 
that transparency, regularity, and simplicity are the characteristics of buildings that are easy to 
disassemble. This implies that load paths need to be easy to identify, the materials and components 
should be similar and repeated in recognizable patterns throughout the building. The variety of 
different material types should be limited, and handling aspects such as dismantling and lifting 
should be considered in the (connection) design. Friction connections should be implemented 
wherever possible as their use avoids cutting holes in connecting members.  
 
The approach of utilizing reclaimed concrete elements in combination with perimeter 
reinforcement, as introduced previously (Fig. 6), was further developed at the building scale by 
(Widmer et al., 2023) who designed an innovative bracing system that incorporates sealed U-
stirrups to create a rigid connection between reclaimed and in-situ concrete (Fig. 7).  
==PAGE:30==
 
15 
 
Fig. 7 
New bracing system with reclaimed concrete walls to resist lateral forces by (Widmer et al., 2023) 
Studio Anne Holtrup developed a double-layer wall concept for a building project in Bahrain (Fig. 8) 
that promotes the preservation of cultural heritage by imitating the destroyed aesthetics using 
concrete elements cast in sand molds to create amorphous borders (‘Anne Holtrop Qaysariyah 
Suq’, 2021). With respect to structural reuse, this approach can help avoid expensive cutting while 
simultaneously ensuring a continuous loading area.  
 
 
 
Fig. 8 
Double-layer approach: Model (Left) and finished building by Studio Anne Holtrup (Right) 
Fig. 9 illustrates a structural reuse pilot house in Mehrow, Germany, where reclaimed concrete walls 
were lifted into position with chains attached to lifting anchors (screwable heavy-lift dowel system). 
Wet connections were created between the horizontal and vertical structural elements.  
 
==PAGE:31==

Another prototype building was constructed as part of a research project at TU Berlin. A high-
pitched roof wall was assembled using diagonally sawn wall elements and shortened floor slabs 
(Fig. 10).

Gellerupparken where floor slabs and wall elements were preserved (Fig. 11).

==PAGE:32==
 
17 
2.3 (DfD) Connections for hollow core walls  
 
Connections are considered critical regions and represent one of the most important aspects of 
structural building design. (Estrella et al., 2024) stated that, in the case of monolithic connections, 
disassembling construction elements may necessitate demolition, thereby jeopardizing the integrity 
of components hindering their further high-quality reuse. Consequently, this research focuses on 
conventional methods for integrating wall elements, as well as innovative approaches that enable 
the easier disassembly of structural components. This shift is supported by emerging policies, such 
as the new London Plan, which mandates that planning applications demonstrate how building 
components can be disassembled and reused (London Plan Guidance: Circular Economy Statements, 
2022). Additionally, initiatives like the EU's Buildings as Material Banks (BAMB) established the 
groundwork for guidelines that promote this design philosophy. 
 
2.3.1 
Functional requirements 
While the Eurocode does not specify requirements for the connection of reused elements, key 
functional demands include air and water tightness, corrosion and fire resistance, the capacity to 
transfer imposed loads with adequate strength, freedom of rotation for hinged connections, 
absorption of creep and shrinkage, and overall durability. In the context of structural reuse, the 
connection’s ability to account for geometrical tolerances is crucial (See interview from p.150). 
(Odenbreit et al., 2022) highlighted that to prevent plastic deformation during the first service life, 
reusable elements and connectors must meet additional serviceability criteria.  
 
When Design for Disassembly (DfD) is required, the principles outlined in ISO 20887:2020 
emphasize the importance of ease of access to components, independence, simplicity, avoidance 
of unnecessary treatments, support for circular economy models, standardization, and safety during 
disassembly. Additional considerations include accommodating dimensional and erection 
tolerances, cost efficiency, production feasibility, and aesthetics. Generally, connections can be 
categorized in various ways, such as by static system and load transfer, as shown in Fig. 12 and can 
be further specified by connection type (wall-wall, wall-foundation, wall-slab). 
 
Fig. 12 Categorizing wall connections by static system 
 
 
 
==PAGE:33==
 
18 
2.3.2 State-of-the-Art  
 
Standard precast connections 
Wet connections are the standard for precast concrete structures and are created on-site by placing 
connecting reinforcement and then pouring mortar between the structural elements to facilitate 
composite action. Typical interstory joints of hollow core panels utilize anchorage with grouted 
sleeves which are not economic and require high machining accuracy (Zhou et al., 2023). (Seifi et 
al., 2019) proposed a new type of wall connection with metal ducts that accommodate for 
tolerances during positioning and subsequent grouting, ensuring a rigid connection. Fig. 13 shows 
the detail of a wet, rigid connection between a load-bearing hollow core panel and a hollow core 
slab with a minimum mortar filling of the vertical hollow cores. Regarding the disassembly of these 
connections, (Heckmann et al., 2024) identified that the primary challenge in deconstructing HCS 
is loosening the conventionally grouted longitudinal joints between the elements without causing 
damage.  
 
 
Fig. 13 Wet connection between hollow core wall and slab (Left) and grouted metal duct connection by (Seifi et al., 2019) 
Dry connections 
A dry joint simplifies the construction process by eliminating the time required for curing, while 
also facilitating deconstruction and reuse. Currently, this method is not commonly used for 
concrete walls, as the incorporation of steel connectors raises concerns regarding fire. Wet joints, 
on the other hand, allow for greater tolerances and enable a uniform distribution of the applied 
load (Luise Dennin & Annika Bang Thomsen, 2023). (Zhou et al., 2023) also noted that DfD 
interstory connections are not as robust as monolithic concrete structures. A brittle failure can 
occur due to the crushing of the HCP. Therefore, detailing is crucial and can approached similar 
to masonry structures by, for example, introducing confining frames consisting of ring beams and 
tie columns (See p. 21).  
 
Bolted or clamped connections are typically used in low seismicity regions while for higher seismic 
risk the use of pre-tensioning has proven to be a feasible solution.(Martins et al., 2023) Loads are 
transferred through compression or friction. Mechanical connections require less labour and 
facilitate easy assembly and disassembly such as the steel connector for walls developed by (Guo 
et al., 2019) (Fig. 14).  
==PAGE:34==
 
19 
 
Fig. 14 Novel bolt connection for precast wall panel structure by (Guo et al., 2019) 
(Bastien-Masse et al., 2024) utilized preloaded threaded rods at the element corners to connect 
reclaimed floor slabs to a steel beam (Fig. 15). A layer of mortar was applied to accommodate for 
tolerances from sawing and to ensure proper contact between the elements. Additionally, epoxy-
glued plastic strips were employed to waterproof the joint.  
 
Fig. 15 Connection of reclaimed concrete floor slabs to steel beam by (Bastien-Masse et al., 2024) 
With regard to lifting and handling HCS and HCP, an anchor systems exists, such as the one shown 
in Fig. 16. It is placed into a pre-drilled hole in the concrete matrix with the specified diameter. As 
a metric screw or threaded rod is inserted and tightened, the cone expands to fit within the hole. 
 
 
Fig. 16 Anchor for hollow core slabs and panels (after casting) 
==PAGE:35==
 
20 
Global connections: Confining frames 
(Ci et al., 2023) developed a construction system for single-story buildings using precast hollow-
core concrete panels reinforced with cast-in-place boundary elements (Fig. 17). Low-cyclic loading 
tests were conducted on three specimens to assess the reliability of the connections between the 
components and to verify their seismic performance.  
 
 
Fig. 17 Study on seismic performance of confined HCP by (Ci et al., 2023) 
The relocatable habitat unit (Fig. 18) is a modular, transportable living space originally designed for 
use in extreme environments, such as military operations, disaster relief, or remote industrial sites. 
Assembly and disassembly are facilitated by the connection design. 
 
Fig. 18 Relocatable Habitat Unit 
==PAGE:36==
 
21 
Global concepts: Post-tensioning 
Post-tensioning provides the wall with an additional compressive load, enhancing its load-bearing 
resistance to horizontal loads caused by wind and earthquakes. Lateral stability can be achieved 
through a frame system, as demonstrated by the project “Ofenturm” in Cham which was designed 
by Boltshauser Architekten. In this project, the load-bearing structure was stabilized by connecting the 
upper timber beam to the foundation using threaded rods and nuts (Fig. 19). Unbonded post-
tensioned concrete columns have already been implemented in regions with significant seismic risk 
(Nikbakht, 2017). This system also accommodates for tolerances without compromising the 
precise positioning of the elements, while channeling steel through the element is beneficial for the 
fire safety. Post-tensioning systems can be tied-off globally (over the full building height) or locally 
(at the interstory level). The latter option offers advantages such as improved construction stability, 
a more efficient load path, reduced drift, and the accommodation of shrinkage at each floor level. 
Anchor tie-down systems effectively direct diaphragm loads from roofs, floors and walls to the 
foundation and are primarily used in timber construction (Simpson Strong Tie, 2025) (Fig. 20). 
 
 
 
Fig. 19 Ofenturm Cham: Post-tensioned rammed earth structure 
 
Fig. 20 Anchor tie-down system for lateral bracing (Here from supplier Simpson Strong-Tie Company) 
==PAGE:37==
 
22 
TAKK Architects designed a pavilion made entirely from recycled building materials, employing a 
post-tensioning system to enable the reuse of concrete blocks. The combination of reclaimed 
concrete elements and a tie system was also showcased in the Master project of Lucas Lamberti 
(Fig. 21). 
 
 
 
Fig. 21 Pavilion by TAKK Architects 2022 (Left) and architectural model of Master project by Lucas Lamberti (Right) 
Selected DfD concrete connections 
The contact to Aarhus Arkitekturskolen, ABC Rådgivende Ingeniører and Lejerbo inspired the collection 
of connections currently studied and already implemented in the field of (structural) reuse. To 
minimize extensive cutting and account for dimensional tolerances, the reuse mock-up (Fig. 22) 
incorporates steel plates, dowels, and angle connectors with slotted holes. Deviations between 
elements are addressed using mortar. Timber elements function as brackets to bridge gaps caused 
by insufficient element height. The structural reuse project Thoravej 29 in Nordvest (Copenhagen) 
repurposed TT floor slabs on-site as ramps for a new staircase. Steel connections for top and 
bottom were specifically designed for the project to account for dimensional tolerances and the 
angle of the ramps. To preserve the prestressing forces within the TT-slabs a steel bracket was 
mounted to the ends before cutting the elements (Fig. 23 and Fig. 24). 
 
 
 
  
Fig. 22 Mock-up at Arkitekturskolen Aarhus, showcasing wall-to-wall connections 
==PAGE:38==
 
23 
 
 
Fig. 23 Bottom connections of ramp at Thoravej 29 (Left) and bracket for TT slab ends (Right) 
 
 
Fig. 24 Bottom connections of ramp at Thoravej 29 (Left) and detail from Circle House: DfD steel connector 
2.4 Feasibility of using hollow core slabs as walls 
 
Reusing slabs as walls (foreign application) has been discussed in design seminars of Asam with the 
goal of achieving higher room heights. This application was considered limited by technology and 
the quality of building components (Asam, 2007). Parabase, a Swiss architecture firm, won a 
competition for a housing development in Walkeweg, Basel, with a design proposal that involves 
the reuse of prefabricated concrete elements (Fig. 25). They proposed using floor slabs as walls 
that span over two stories. The realization is still pending. 
 
==PAGE:39==
 
24 
 
 
Fig. 25 Parabase proposal for housing in Basel: Reuse of prefabricated concrete elements 
Difference between HCS, HCP and HCSW 
Hollow core panels (HCP) are standardized vertical concrete elements with continuous, 
longitudinal voids to reduce their dead load. Typically, these precast elements are prestressed on 
both sides to enhance rigidity and crack resistance. HCP differ from HCS as the latter generally 
only incorporate prestressing strands at the bottom. Consequently, HCP do not have a precamber. 
HCSW are HCS that are repurposed as walls. They experience the same loading conditions as HCP 
but differ with respect to the reinforcement layout. 
 
2.4.1 
State-of-the-Art: Integration and structural behaviour of hollow core panels 
 
 
 
Fig. 26 Load-bearing construction with load-bearing hollow core panels (Left), From HCS to HCSW (Right) 
Hollow core panels (HCP) have been utilized in precast construction, including in Denmark, but 
they are not as prevalent as hollow core slabs (HCS). Often, the panels are confined by steel beams 
at top and bottom, along with tie rods to improve their strength, stability, and ductility (Zhou et 
al., 2023). Unlike most precast systems, reinforced hollow core panels typically span continuously 
from the foundation to the roof to maintain flexural integrity (S.K. Ghosh, 2015). (Hamid & 
Mander, 2010) implemented both structural and non-structural prestressed HCP in an experimental 
set-up to investigate their lateral seismic resistance, assuming their application in a single-story 
warehouse building. The structural walls were anchored to the foundation using unbonded post-
tensioning tendons, while the non-structural elements, which served as cladding, were placed 
directly on rubber pads above the foundation (Fig. 27).  
 
==PAGE:40==
 
25 
 
Fig. 27 Testing set-up with structural and non-structural HCP by (Hamid & Mander, 2010) 
Load path 
The design approach for buildings utilizing hollow core wall panels is akin to that of other precast 
concrete walls, as a load path must be established to transfer all design loads – both gravitational 
and lateral – from their point of application to their ultimate point of resistance (S.K. Ghosh, 2015). 
Lateral loads encompass horizontal bracing forces resulting from eccentric dead loads, as well as 
wind and seismic loads acting perpendicular to the wall face. 
 
Detailing 
(Zhou et al., 2023) state that HCPs can exhibit brittle failure when subjected to axial load, induced 
by the crushing of the panel. Therefore, it is recommended to design the interstory connections 
similarly to those of masonry structures, e.g. with confining frames that can prevent excessive 
structural deformation and sudden collapse of the elements.  
 
Behaviour under axial load 
(Zhou et al., 2023) tested prestressed HCP with a height of 2.51 m, a monolithic connection to 
foundation and ring beam (total height including the upper ring beam: ca. 3 m) under axial loading. 
Longitudinal cracks were observed, and it was noted that the cracking pattern depended on the 
shape of the hollow cores. Under pure axial loading, no out-of-plane deformation and stability 
failure were observed. As illustrated in Fig. 28, localized damage occurred at both ends, and single 
or multiple longitudinal full-height cracks were identified. Under out-of-plane loading, the panels 
exhibited ductile behaviour, with cracks concentrating at the bottom joint while the panels 
remained undamaged. In conclusion, (Zhou et al., 2023) determined that the global stability of 
HCP with normal story heights can be assured with no stability failures occurring during axial load 
tests. 
 
Fig. 28 HCP under axial loading, tested until failure (Zhou et al., 2023) 
==PAGE:41==
 
26 
Openings 
Openings in walls create geometric discontinuities that result in non-linear stress distributions 
which can diminish the ultimate load capacity and stiffness. According to (Ho et al., 2020), walls 
are typically divided into segments of columns connected by cross beams at the openings. The PCI 
manual indicates that for small openings (size not further defined), the remaining cross section may 
compensate for the concrete and reinforcement that have been removed due to the opening. A 
frame becomes necessary when the opening reaches or exceeds the width of the precast panel. It 
takes up the dead load of the panel and transfers the lateral loads from the cut part to adjacent wall 
elements (S.K. Ghosh, 2015). 
 
Global considerations 
Connections between the walls and the roof must be established to transfer the wall panel reactions 
into the roof structure and, if necessary, to transfer diaphragm forces from the roof. 
==PAGE:42==
 
27 
3 Sourcing and primary assessment of hollow core slabs 
 
 
 
Storage of HCS at P. Olesen 
3 Sourcing and 
    Primary Assessment 
    of HCS 
==PAGE:43==

3.1 
Origin of hollow core slabs 
 
The Danish demolition company P. Olesen provided five concrete hollow core slabs that originate 
from an industrial building in Aarhus, were they were dismantled in April 2020 and since then 
stored outside underneath a canopy roof at P. Olesen’s company site in Hovedgaard (Jylland). The 
elements were delivered in September 2024 to DTU in Lyngby and prepared for the experimental 
tests. They were stored outside and supported by two timber posts per slab. 
 
 
 
Fig. 29 Delivery of HCS from P. Olesen in Hovedgaard to DTU Concrete Lab (Left) and storage (Right) 
3.1.1 
Element labels and evaluation of historical documentation 
In order to differentiate between the different elements, they were labeled PO_1A/B-PO_05_A/B 
with a marker (Fig. 30). The documentation of the element’s history was eased as an original label 
from the precast factory was still attached to one of the elements. Aarhus’ building archive gave 
access to detailed information including original position of the elements in the donor building and 
the structural calculations. The latter provided information about material strengths and previous 
loading. The original producer, Spæncom, was contacted to receive more information about the 
prestressing strands which they provided with plans of the exact elements and the tensile test 
diagrams for the two different prestressing strand types used (See Appendix Fig. A 13, Fig. A 14 
and Fig. A 15). 
 
 
 
Fig. 30 Original label from Spæncom (Left) and labeling the HCS elements at DTU (Right) 
 
 
==PAGE:44==
 
29 
3.2 Visual assessment 
 
3.2.1 
Element condition 
When using the assessment scheme proposed by (Devènes et al., 2023) which is illustrated in Table 
2, the elements are classified as grade 4 (Bad condition) due to local but noticeable damage in the 
anchor regions with holes in the concrete matrix of up to ca. 15 mm diameter next to the steel 
anchors (Fig. 31). The damage results from the dismantling process of the donor structure as the 
anchors had to be freed to allow for the lifting of the elements. According to the reuse assessment 
scheme, the elements should not be reused as load-bearing elements without appropriate repair 
measures. 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 31 Damage detection of HCS: Anchor regions and uneven, upper surface (layer of leveling mortar) 
3.2.2 Reinforcement layout 
Each HCS contains 8 low-relaxation, prestressing steel strands of type Y1860C and Y1860S7 as 
well as slack transversal reinforcement of type K8 at the ends. Some specimens showed an 
additional longitudinal reinforcement bar that was previously used for the longitudinal joints 
between the slabs. The destructive tests led to the partial freeing of the strands which eased the 
identification of their type (Fig. 32). It was found that the reinforcement layout differs from the 
plan drawing and static (Fig. 32 and Fig. A 11) with respect to the position and number of the two 
different strand types L5 (monostrand) and L9.3 (7 wires). Instead of 4 strands of each type, the 
slabs inherit two strands of type L5 and six of type L9.3 which has an influence on the total 
prestressing force. Corrosion of the reinforcement was only detected at the ends that were exposed 
to weathering during the storage. The tensile test diagrams of the prestressing strands from the 
producer as well as the plan drawing can be found in the Appendix (Fig. A 13-Fig. A 15). 
 
The remaining prestressing force was estimated according to the reinforcement layout found 
during the visual inspection. A loss of 20% due to relaxation and creep was considered.

1992-1-1 + AC: Eurocode 2: Design of Concrete Structures - Part 1-1: General Rules and Rules for Buildings, 
2008) for new concrete elements was consulted to investigate whether the HCSW fulfill the 
requirements for the minimum reinforcement. 
 
In section 9.5.2 in Eurocode 2 (DS/EN 1992-1-1 + AC: Eurocode 2: Design of Concrete Structures - Part 
1-1: General Rules and Rules for Buildings, 2008), the minimum amount of longitudinal reinforcement

4 Material testing 
 
Concrete close up pic 
 
 
 
 
4 Material Testing 
==PAGE:47==
 
32 
Investigating and documenting the material properties of reclaimed reinforced concrete elements, 
such as elasticity, compressive and flexural strength, is essential to predict their behavior under 
loading. Comprehensive material testing was carried out in collaboration with Skivik, Master 
student of Civil Engineering at DTU, and covered non-destructive and destructive methods in 
order to assess strength and durability of the concrete (See Nora Johanne Førde Skivik, 2025). The 
stochastic analysis of the results was discussed considering factors like specimen dimensions, 
material defects, and testing conditions.  
 
Expected material strength from existing documentation  
Prior to the tests, the structural calculations of the donor building were assessed to collect 
information on the concrete strength. In the material section, two concrete types of different 
strengths are defined: strengbeton and Spanmax overpart with a respective characteristic compressive 
strength of 45 MPa and 35 MPa (See Fig. 33). 
 
 
Fig. 33 Material information provided in the structural calculations 
This led to the assumption that the hollow core slabs have a characteristic compressive strength of 
at least 45 MPa with an additional top layer of concrete with a minimum characteristic strength of 
35 MPa. To verify the material information, Spæncom‘s technical manager, Finn Passov, was 
contacted. He clarified that in the structural calculations, strengbeton only refers to beams and TT 
slabs and provided the following information that was not included in the structural design report: 
 
“Hollow core elements produced in Ålborg at that time [2006] had a bottom layer of approximately 
50 mm of concrete with fck = 45 MPa, a middle layer of fck = 35 MPa concrete and a thin top layer 
of mortar rated for 45 MPa. The strands will be with 7 wires and a strength of 1860 MPa, low 
relaxation.” (Mail from 14.01.2025)  
 
Fig. 34 Cross section of HCS PX22: Concrete layers with different compressive strengths 
==PAGE:48==
 
33 
The different material layers are illustrated in Fig. 34, indicating that the majority of the element, 
approximately 60% of the cross section, is constituted of concrete with a characteristic compressive 
strength of 35 MPa.  
 
For the original calculation of the HCS by the producer, the top layer was omitted due to its variable 
height, which serves as leveling mortar for the precamber. The higher strength bottom layer 
(45 MPa) was utilized to increase the bonding of the strands as well as the capacity of the cracked 
cross section in the deflection calculation of the slabs. While the top layer could be identified after 
the cutting of the slabs ends, the difference between middle and bottom layer is not visible without 
further knowledge (Fig. 35). The acquisition of quality control data from the exact production line 
of the HCS was not possible due to a system overload on the part of Spæncom. 
 
 
Fig. 35 Material strength indicated in structural calculations and provided by Spæncom for reclaimed elements after contact in 01/2025 
 
 
==PAGE:49==
 
34 
4.1 
Non-destructive testing methods 
 
4.1.1 
Rebound hammer test 
The rebound hammer (Schmidt hammer) testing and the conversion to the associated compressive 
strength were carried out according to DS/EN 12504-2:2021, DS/S-328:2021, and 
DS/EN 13791:2019 (Table B.2), respectively. It is an indirect, non-destructive testing method to 
estimate the compressive strength of concrete. The rebound index of the impact weight measures 
the surface hardness of the material. A correlation with destructive compression tests of the same 
material and location should be carried out to ensure the accuracy and reliability of the results 
(Katja Irene Møller Thygesen, 2023). 
 
Each slab was tested 60 times in total on the upper, cleaned surface with a rebound hammer of 
type “SilverSchmidt 8200, Type N” (Fig. 36). Five different testing areas with a testing grid of 12 
measuring points were marked on each slab (Fig. 37). The total rebound number per slab was 
recommended by PhD students Katja Irene Møller Thygesen and Serkan Karatosun from the research 
group StructuralReuse at DTU Sustain. The distance between the measuring points adheres to the 
minimum distance outlined in the standard.  
 
 
 
Fig. 36 Rebound hammer (Left) and testing grid 12 cm x 12 cm (Right) 
 
Fig. 37 Testing areas for rebound hammer 
 
 
==PAGE:50==
 
35 
4.1.2 
Phenolphthalein test 
If the concrete that surrounds the steel strands is carbonated over time, it loses its ability to protect 
the steel from corrosion thanks to its alkaline properties. An indicator for the deterioration level 
of concrete is the carbonation depth which can be measured with the phenolphthalein test. The 
FCRBE Reuse Toolkit states that this check is necessary for the structural reuse of walls. If the 
carbonation depth exceeds the concrete cover depth divided by two, a mortar repair becomes 
necessary. (Belgian Building Research Institute et al., 2021) For the tested HCS this equals to 
approximately 15 mm. The Norwegian standard for the reuse of HCS permits carbonation depths 
of up to 10 mm. Higher values require a separate assessment of the remaining service life (Standards 
Norway, 2022). 
 
The phenolphthalein test method according to EN 14630 is a colorimetric test using a 1% ethyl-
alcohol-phenolphthalein solution intended to measure the depth of the carbonated layer near the 
surface of hardened concrete. The solution changes its color from colorless to purple when the pH 
value exceeds 9.2. Applied onto the cut concrete surface, it will turn purple in the non-carbonated 
areas, thereby indicating an alkaline environment. The transition between carbonated and non-
carbonated areas is not clear-cut. Consequently, it is up to the subjectivity of the operator to 
establish the transition perimeter. The carbonation depth was measured on all HCS at the slab end 
after the diamond saw cutting by applying several drops of the solution onto the concrete surface. 
 
4.2 Destructive testing methods 
 
The compressive and flexural strength were tested in the structural lab by another Master student. 
In this report, the description of methods and testing procedures is kept to a minimum while a 
comprehensive report was issued by Skivik (Nora Johanne Førde Skivik, 2025).  
 
4.2.1 
Extraction and storage of specimens 
According to the Danish standard DS/EN 12390-1, the concrete compressive strength can be 
determined on core drillings with diameters of 100 mm or 150 mm and height-to-diameter ratios 
h/d of 2/1 or 1/1. Alternatively, cubes with an edge length of 150 mm can be tested. Due to the 
geometry of the HCS, the standard could not be followed. 
 
The Norwegian standard for the reuse of HCS, NS 3682:2021, proposes to test 4 samples from 
two different elements per batch of 20 HCS. Cylindrical core samples with a minimum diameter 
of 50 mm should be extracted along the cut end if samples cannot be taken from top or bottom. 
It is recommended to extract cores with a bigger diameter of at least 60 mm, which was not possible 
in the case of the reclaimed elements for this project. The company Herlevdiamanten, that was 
responsible for cutting the HCS, also extracted two concrete cores per slab with a diameter of 
45 mm. A standard core of 70 mm or 100 mm according to the Danish standard for compressive 
strength tests, DS/EN 12390-1, was not possible to extract due to the HCS geometry (See the 
limited area for core drilling in Fig. A 23). PhD student Serkan Karatosun from DTU’s StructuralReuse 
research group who specializes in material testing of reclaimed concrete elements said that a 
diameter to a minimum height ratio 2:1 for the core specimens is desirable which was adhered to 
in the case of this project. Lower ratios may overestimate the concrete compressive strength.  
 
Fig. 38 shows the location of the core extraction as well as the concrete piece extracted from 
PO_3B. The latter was used for the cutting of cube and cuboid samples for compressive and 
flexural strength tests (Fig. 41). The specimens were labelled after their extraction and stored in a 
climate room (20°C, 50-85% RH). Before testing they were cut with a diamond saw to achieve a 
straight load application surface (Fig. 39). The height of the core samples ranged from 65-100 mm 
(Table A 1). 
 
==PAGE:51==
 
36 
 
Fig. 38 Extraction of core samples from cut-off pieces and cut out between hollow cores for cube samples (Specimen PO_3) 
 
 
Fig. 39 Storage of core samples after extraction and cut samples before testing 
The 8 cube samples (ID 1-8) had an approximate edge length of 50 mm, while the remaining 7 
specimens (ID 9-15) for the compression tests had varying dimensions and were finally not 
considered in the analysis of the test results. For the flexural strength tests, 8 cuboids were cut out 
of the same concrete piece (Fig. 41) whose dimensions are given in Table A 1 and are slightly 
smaller than specified by DS/EN 12390-1.  
 
 
==PAGE:52==

Fig. 40 Cube samples from PO_3B 
 
 
Fig. 41 Prisms from PO_3B for flexural strength tests  
4.2.2 Compression test 
Destructive compression tests increase the knowledge level of the material properties. The purpose 
was to provide a better estimate of the remaining load-bearing capacity of the HCS and investigate 
their ability to withstand crushing under compression. In total, 10 cylindrical core samples and 15 
cubes were tested according to DS/EN 12390-2 and DS/EN 13791 with a EME-083 compression 
testing machine in the structural lab of DTU aligning with DS/EN 12390-4.  
 
 
Fig. 42 Core specimens for compression tests 
 
 
==PAGE:53==
 
38 
Prior to the loading, the specimens were prepared with a mesotite sheet at top and bottom surface 
to minimize the influence of misalignment which can affect the results. From the individual values 
for the compressive strength of the specimens, the mean value, the standard deviation as well as 
the characteristic compressive strength were calculated. The latter considers the variation of the 
test results and indicates the 5% quantile of the compressive strength, i.e. the minimum 
compressive strength achieved by 95% of the concrete samples. The characteristic value for the 
concrete compressive strength was calculated according to the standard EN 13791 (Assessment of 
in-situ compressive strength in structures and precast concrete components) (Knab & Sodeikat, 2015) with 
methods A and B:

4.2.3 Flexural strength test 
The flexural strength measures a material’s ability to resist bending under a load. Prism-shaped 
specimens were tested in a three-point bending configuration which complies with DS/EN 12390-
4 (Fig. 43). The test was conducted according to DS/EN 12390-5. Adjustments to the specimen 
shape were made after trial tests, as the jointing mortar on the prism edges affected the results with 
weak bonding leading to shear failure (See Fig. 44). Consequently, the mortar edges were removed 
leading to shorter specimens that exhibited a more uniform behavior under loading and bending 
failure (Fig. 45). Note that the curved loading surface of the specimen is due to the hollow core 
geometry.

The failure mechanisms of cubes and cores align with the speciﬁcations from the Eurocode that 
indicate cracking and splitting modes for specimens that are subjected to high compressive forces

4.4 Discussion 
 
From the visual assessment, the concrete matrix seemed to be in good condition which was further 
supported by the results from the phenolphthalein test indicating a sufficient alkaline layer over the 
reinforcement. The condition at element scale was found to be bad due to significant damage of 
the anchor regions with holes protruding into the hollow cores.  
 
4.4.1 
Compressive strength 
The results from rebound hammer and destructive tests do not match the documentation from the 
building archive. For the top layer, the non-destructive tests indicated a concrete class C20/25 for 
all five slabs that were tested, which is inferior to the expected characteristic compressive strength 
of 45 MPa. According to Serkan Karatosun, PhD student of the StructuralReuse group at DTU, the 
result can be affected by an insufficient bond between top layer and main matrix. This 
interpretation is supported by the failure mode of specimen #3 after the flexural strength test (Fig. 
51). The destructive compression tests of two core samples from each slab and cube samples from 
one slab, resulted in similar characteristic concrete compressive strengths of ca. 20 MPa which is 
considerably inferior the expected value of minimum 35 MPa. Usually, the core strength can be 
converted to the cube strength by applying a factor of 0.8 (Brencich et al., 2021). The conversion 
was not found to be suitable, as the mean cube strength value exceeds the core strength with 30 
MPa compared to 27 MPa. (Brencich et al., 2021) found that damage to the concrete core from 
drilling and cutting as well as the core diameter affects the results for historical more than for 
modern concrete. They detected that the core-to-cubic strength ratio decreased as a result. This 
can explain the measured values and why the common conversion factor is not applicable. A 
standard deviation of 5% for the cores and 7% for the cubes was observed. (Brencich et al., 2021) 
note that in-situ tests of concrete structures with core drillings can lead to results with a 
considerable error of up to ± 20%. With respect to the characteristic strength this would indicate 
a range of 16-24 MPa as compressive strength for the HCS.  
 
Overall, the destructive material testing of the specimen samples can confirm homogeneity only to 
a limited extent as the variation in the results is considerable. This highlights the difficulty of 
obtaining compressive strength values that represent the material’s original or potential 
performance. The variations in the results might not be due to actual differences in the strength of 
the different elements but can result from: 
 
• Extraction of samples for testing from used concrete elements: 
o Cutting imperfections 
o Limited number of valid specimens 
o Different specimen dimensions 
o Core length factor differed: Ratio diameter to height of specimen 
o Minor defects of the concrete matrix of the cube specimens e.g. porosity, small 
cracks (Fig. 40), spalled-off corners 
o Variable size and distribution of aggregates 
o Specimen preparation is different from the standard: Extrusion for HCS, casting 
for standard samples 
• Testing-related: 
o Misalignment: Partially angled surface for loading, possibly insufficient adjustment 
of machine head  
o Used mesotite sheets were applied to top and bottom of the samples (not new as 
recommended by the standard) 
o Use of an overly powerful testing machine such as EME-083 can cause immediate 
failure of weaker specimens or introduce additional defects during testing. 
==PAGE:60==
 
45 
4.4.2 Flexural strength 
In case of the flexural strength tests, the changes in span length due to the removal of weak mortar 
had an impact on stress distribution and failure modes. Local weaknesses became critical during 
testing and led to shear failure. After the adjustment, the failure mode aligned with the one 
described in the standard and the average flexural strength of the valid specimens was calculated 
as 5.4 MPa, still with a considerable standard deviation of 38%. This highlights the influence of 
factors such as specimen dimensions, surface defects, and inconsistencies during preparation. 
Overall, the results of the flexural bending tests lack significance given the combination of:  
 
• A small number of specimens (5 in total, thereby neglecting specimens that have shown 
shear failure) 
• The variation in specimen dimensions (d1, d2 and L): Every specimen had other dimensions 
and jointing mortar as well as the curved surface due to the hollow core geometry affected 
the results. 
• The variation in the loading rate (different for every specimen ranging from 38-59,52 N/s) 
• A significant standard deviation of 38% in the results 
 
 
==PAGE:61==
 
46 
5 Axial load-bearing capacity of HCS-walls: Analytical and 
numerical investigation  
 
 
 
5 Axial load-bearing  
    capacity  
 
    Analytical and  
    numerical investigation  
 
==PAGE:62==
 
47 
Initial remarks 
Structural reuse calls for analytical methods that allow for a conservative estimate of the remaining 
load-bearing capacity of the HCSW, proven by experimental testing. Different approaches were 
used to define a range of lower and upper bound solutions for the ultimate vertical load. Generally, 
the bearing capacity of walls depend on concrete strength, wall thickness, wall height, 
reinforcement, the eccentricity of vertical forces as well as the condition for the load transfer (rigid, 
semi-rigid, hinged). 
 
Here, it was studied how the extent of the activated area for the load distribution affects the 
ultimate vertical load, varying from a concentrated load application through the webs of the steel 
connection to full activation of the idealized cross section (Fig. 52). Consequently, the chapter was 
divided in theories describing the uniformly distributed loading (5.1) as well as concentrated loading 
of unreinforced concrete blocks (5.2). The existing reinforcement in the slabs is neglected in the 
calculations as it is only subjected to compression during the tests (See also Fig. 76).  
 
Fig. 52 Range of the activated cross section for the load application 
All calculations consider a HCSW with a width of 600 mm and a height of 3200 mm as this 
resembles the specimens’ dimensions (cut in half in the longitudinal direction). With regards to the 
stability of the wall, these are conservative assumptions given that in reality, the width would be 
minimum 1200 mm (standard width of HCS) while the room height in new buildings is usually 
lower than 3200 mm. For the material properties, a concrete C35/45 according to Eurocode 1 
(Table A 2) is assumed if not stated differently. This is a conservative assumption as the bottom 
and top layer of the slab, with 50 mm and ca. 10 mm respectively, have a higher compressive 
strength of minimum 45 MPa (See Fig. 34). The detailed geometrical and material properties of the 
reclaimed HCS can be found in the Appendix p.173ff. The mechanical damage of the elements 
was not considered in the calculations. They can however have an influence on the results of the 
experimental tests as described in section 6.4.1. In the following the analytical methods are 
explained in detail while in 5.3 an overview of the results is presented. 
 
The mathematics software Maple (Version 2023.0) was used for the calculations.

calculation of the failure load of columns. Failure can be due to concrete crushing or due to 
buckling depending on the column height. Given the ratio width to height of the HCSW specimens, 
the method can be applied.

5.1.3 
Mohr-Coulomb: Elasto-Plastic calculation for prismatic bodies under compression 
 
By using the theory of Mohr-Coulomb and implementing their elasto-plastic material model, the 
failure load as well as the angle of fracture of concrete under normal and shear stress can be 
described with the cohesion c and the angle of friction φ. The angle of fracture β is constituted by 
the end surfaces of the prism and the yield plane (See Fig. 53). Concrete is suitable for the 
application of the theory as it is a brittle material with a compressive strength exceeding by far its 
tensile strength. 
 
Fig. 53 Failure mechanisms of a prismatic body under compression (M.P. Nielsen & L.C. Hoang, 2010) 
Nielsen and Hoang proposed a model for the determination of an upper bound solution of the 
uniformly distributed compressive stress p on a prismatic body assuming a Coulomb material 
(M.P. Nielsen & L.C. Hoang, 2010). 
 
==PAGE:66==
 
51

corresponds to a theoretical, maximum vertical load of 2079 kN and 2362 kN, assuming a load 
distribution over the entire cross section of the HCSW (Area Ac = 78.150 mm2).
