5.2.2 Strut-and-tie model 
A strut-and-tie model (STM) is a calculation model developed by (J. Schlaich & K. Schäfer, 1991) 
which is used for the scenario of a concentrated load application and leads to a bottle-shaped 
compressive stress field (Fig. 57). The applicability of the model is limited due to the hollow core 
geometry of the slab. The load propagation in HCSW can only be sufficiently described in a 3-
dimensional way as the force flow is disturbed by the hollow cores. Here, the STM serves as a 
simplified method to model the HCSW’s behaviour. 
 
Fig. 57 Strut-tie-model for wall according to (J. Schlaich & K. Schäfer, 1991): Model, stress fields and node regions  
(J. Schlaich & K. Schäfer, 2001) stated for concentrated loading, the force propagation causes two- 
or three-axial compression which leads to tensile stresses. The lack of transverse reinforcement can 
in combination with the longitudinal, compressive stresses lead to early failure. Consequently, the 
ultimate failure load of unreinforced concrete walls depends on the tensile strength of the concrete 
and its capacity to redistribute the stress after the occurrence of one or more longitudinal cracks. 
The HCSW is assumed to be unreinforced as a only a single transversal reinforcement bar (K8) 
exists at each end of the HCS and for the experiments, the top part of the HCSW including the 
transverse reinforcement were cut off.  
 
The underlying concrete strength depends on the state and the direction of the cracks: 
 
State I: Uncracked 
 
 
fck 
Characteristic concrete compressive strength (35 MPa) 
 
fcd 
Design compressive strength (19.83 MPa) 
 
==PAGE:69==
 
54 
 
State II: Cracked 
 
 
fcd, eff 
Design compressive strength in case of cracks or transverse tension (13.88 MPa) 
 
Cracking load under the influence of the load application width 
As a lower bound, a conservative cracking load can be derived from a linear-elastic calculation if no 
significant constraint or residual stresses are present. The load at which a separation crack occurs, 
depends on the width of the load application a (Fig. 58) (J. Schlaich & K. Schäfer, 2001). The 
concrete is assumed to be uncracked and unreinforced with a characteristic strength of 35 MPa.  
 
In the ideal case, a fully activated cross section, the elastic load corresponds to 1549 kN (highlighted 
in pink in the diagram). If the load application area is reduced, a steep drop in the accountable 
pressing stress can be seen for the graph representing uncracked, reinforced concrete (State I). At 
a = b/2 = 300 mm, a crack can already occur at a pressing stress of 55% fcd (11.82 MPa). The 
maximum vertical load for the elastic state then equals 426 kN (indicated in blue in Fig. 58), while 
for a = 160 mm, the cracking load is calculated as 289 kN. 
 
Fig. 58 Conservative calculation of maximum elastic load (cracking load) depending on load application width, theory by Schlaich Schäfer 
After the first longitudinal crack, the load is redistributed onto the split concrete bodies. For state 
II (cracked), the maximum design strength is reduced by 30% (fcd,eff = 0.7 * fcd) according to Schlaich 
and Schäfer. The effective design compressive strength for the cracked state fcd,eff is then 13.88 MPa. 
 
Assuming for a upper bound solution that after the first longitudinal crack the load is distributed 
uniformly over the cross section of the remaining concrete bodies, the ultimate failure load Nu 
would equal to 1084 kN: 
 
𝑁- = 𝑓1<,=88  ∙𝐴1 
(17) 
 
 
 
 
fcd,eff 
 
Design strength for cracked state: 13.83 MPa (fck = 35 MPa) 
 
Ac 
 
Ideal cross section of HCSW: 78150 mm2 
 
==PAGE:70==
 
55 
To conclude, for state I (uncracked) the cracking load ranges from 426 kN (a = b/2) to 1549 kN 
(a = b) under the consideration of the design compressive strength fcd of a concrete class C35 while 
after cracking (State II), the maximum load is calculated as 1084 kN using the effective compressive 
strength fcd,eff. 
 
Force propagation: Two strut and tie models 
Two strut-and-tie models were developed that study the load application through the steel 
connections at the specimen ends. Model 1 showcases the concentrated load application through 
the two central flanges (t = 20 mm) of the steel connection with a total assumed supporting width 
of 160 mm which equals twice the distance between the center lines of the flanges (See Fig. 59). 
Model 2 studies the load propagation for the activation of all 4 flanges of the top connection (See 
Fig. 60). 
 
Fig. 59 Strut-and-tie model: Two scenarios for the load application through the top steel connection 
The struts were drawn respecting a minimum angle of 45° for the compression struts and the 
horizontal positioning of the tension struts at a maximum distance of 300 mm (b/2) from the load 
application. It was further assumed that the spherical hinges at the bottom of the wall specimen in 
the experiments allow for a uniform load distribution over the cross section.

The diagram shown in Fig. 64 illustrates that the tensile stress that can be accounted for by an 
unreinforced concrete wall is limited to fcd/15 according to (J. Schlaich & K. Schäfer, 2001). This is 
true up until the longitudinal compressive stresses do not exceed 50% of the design compressive 
strength fcd. When exceeding 50%, the compressive stress declines depending on the tensile stresses 
present in the wall. 
 
 
 
Fig. 64 Geometry of the stress field and failure criterion for unreinforced stress field 
The design compressive strength fcd is 19.83 MPa. Consequently, the tension criterion results as 
fcd/15 = 1.32 MPa. Assuming this value for fctm in formula 21, the maximum vertical force F 
applicable to not exceed the tensile capacity is 562 kN (For a load application width a = 160 mm). 
If a is 300 mm, the tensile capacity is exceeded at a vertical load F of 825 kN. For the case of the 
HCSW, a diagram was developed (Fig. 65) that shows this criterion depending on the width of the 
load application. If the latter increases, the force of the tension strut decreases.

5.3 Buckling of split-off column 
 
Due to the lack of transverse reinforcement, the concrete body can split under axial compression 
(Fig. 66). The buckling risk of the split-off column was assessed. 
 
Fig. 66 Illustration of split-off column and its cross section 
The formulae for Euler load, Rankine load as well as ultimate compressive strength have been 
introduced previously (5.1.2). In the case of the split-off column, a characteristic concrete 
compressive strength of 35 MPa and a Young’s Modulus of 34 GPa were assumed. Other relevant 
geometrical factors are presented in Table 7.

The area of the split-off column accounts for ca. 23.7% of the total cross section of the specimen 
(Ac = 78’150 mm2). For in-plane buckling, around the y-axis and therefore in the weak direction, 
the Euler load is calculated as 562 kN, while for the out-of-plane buckling (around the x-axis, 
strong direction), it is 2886 kN. The ultimate compressive strength of the split-off column is 
calculated as 648 kN. Consequently, the buckling load is decisive for the failure in the weak 
direction, while for the strong axis, the compressive strength is greater than the buckling load. The 
Rankin load, that considers both Euler load and compressive strength, was calculated as 301 kN 
for the weak direction, and as 529 kN for the strong direction.

When neglecting eccentricity and precamber, the ultimate compressive strength (linear-elastic) for 
an idealized cross section ranges as a function of the compressive strength from 2735 kN (fck) to 
1550 kN (fcd) in the uncracked state, and to 1085 kN (fcd,eff) for the cracked state (reduction of 30% 
and 60%, respectively). The values decrease proportionally to the activated cross-sectional area. 
When calculating the cracking load (elastic) according to Schlaich Schäfer, the values range from 426 
kN to 1549 kN depending on the load concentration. After cracking the ultimate load is calculated 
as 1084 kN. The investigation of two strut-and-tie models, developed according to the testing 
setup, resulted in maximum peak loads for the more conservative model 1 of 1417 kN (with fck) 
and 802 kN (with fcd), assuming an uncracked state. Depending on the choice of cohesion and 
angle of friction, the Mohr-Coulomb theory (Elasto-plastic approach) approximates the ultimate load 
as 2079-2362 kN. When applying the plastic theory by Nielsen and Hoang for concentrated loading 
on unreinforced concrete, the peak load ranges from 514-1367 kN. The values depend on the 
height of the specimen, the loading width and the value for the compressive strength.  
==PAGE:77==
 
62 
When considering that the strip load is near the edge due to the limited specimen width, the 
ultimate load results as 1732 kN. The Rankin method (empirical formula) is the only method that 
considers eccentricity resulting from the precamber and the eccentric loading. Fig. 67 shows the 
results of the Rankin method in relation to the ultimate compressive strength depending on the 
extent of the activated cross-sectional loading area to investigate probable failure mechanisms, pure 
concrete crushing and combination of crushing and buckling, respectively. The Rankin load is in 
any case more conservative with the highest peak load for the centric case of 2300 kN (ca. 16% 
smaller than the ultimate compressive strength at 100% cross-sectional activation: 2735 kN). The 
total, theoretical load-bearing capacity considerably decreases with rising load eccentricity: At 55 
mm load eccentricity and when additionally considering a precamber of 10 mm, the Rankin load is 
calculated as 940 kN (assuming an idealized, undamaged cross section). This would correspond to 
an ultimate compressive strength of only a third (34%) of the cross section. It is hereby shown that 
the consideration of eccentricities significantly lower the theoretical, ultimate load-bearing capacity.

5.5 Numerical simulation in AutoCAD Inventor 
 
With Autodesk Inventor Professional only linear-elastic material behaviour can be modeled. 
Consequently, the ultimate load of the specimens was not determined quantitatively with the finite 
element modeling software. It was rather used to qualitatively show the load distribution and 
concentration over the cross section of the element at top and bottom considering the type of steel 
connection applied at the ends. The effectiveness of the numerical model is discussed through a 
comparison with the results from experimental testing of HCSW with respect to the cracking 
patterns. It was shown with a simple hand calculation that the steel used for top and bottom 
connection would not start yielding under the applied load. Its strength was conservatively assumed 
as fy = 235 MPa which is not exceeded by the expected failure load from the analytical 
considerations (See Fig. A 26). 
 
5.5.1 
Finite element models  
 
Geometry 
The dimensions of the HCSW model correspond to those of the full-scale specimens that were 
tested in the structural lab with a height of 3.2 m and the idealized cross section shown in Fig. 68. 
The specimen was modeled as a 3D volume in Rhino 7 and then exported as a .stp-file to be 
imported in Autodesk Inventor Version 2025.1. 
 
Fig. 68 Cross section of the FE model (neglecting reinforcement): Half of a HCS 
Connections 
Four FE models were developed to investigate the influence of the steel connection type and the 
eccentricity of the load application on the experimental results: Two with the originally planned 
connection, the other two models with the adapted connection for the forklift (Fig. 69). The applied 
load differed for the load application scenarios: 1500 kN were chosen for the centric case and 
1200 kN for the eccentric case (55 mm). The values correspond to the approximate maximum 
vertical load from the experimental tests for group C and E55 respectively.  
 
The four numerical models (Fig. 70) were labelled as follows: 
 
• CM_C  
Crane model, centric load application, N = 1500 kN 
• CM_E55 
Crane model, eccentric load application, N = 1200 kN 
• FM_C  
Fork lift model, centric load application, N = 1500 kN 
• FM_E55 
Fork lift model, eccentric load application, N = 1200 kN 
 
==PAGE:79==
 
64 
 
Fig. 69 Connections: Original set-up (for transport with crane) and new connection design for forklift 
Loading scenarios 
The load eccentricity was applied by positioning the specimen and its top and bottom connections 
relative to the loading cells (colored in green, Fig. 70). 
 
 
Fig. 70 Front and side view of FE models in Autodesk Inventor: CM_C, CM_E55, FM_C, FM_E55 
==PAGE:80==
 
65 
Material properties 
The characteristic compressive strength was assumed as 35 MPa for the model. This is a 
conservative assumption as parts of the HCS section have a higher characteristic compressive 
strength of 45 MPa which was neglected. The tensile strength and the Young’s modulus were 
derived from Eurocode 2. For the connection parts, a steel class S235 with material properties 
according to Eurocode 3 was chosen on the safe side. The information for concrete and steel is 
summarized in Table 9 and Table 10.

Mesh 
The mesh was refined with an element size of maximum 10 mm to ensure accurate results but limit 
the simulation time (Fig. 71). A mesh refinement analysis was conducted on model CM_C and can 
be found in the Appendix (From p. 187). 
 
 
Fig. 71 Meshed specimen (Close up cross section) in Autodesk Inventor Professional 
Boundary conditions 
The loading cell was assumed to be fixed at the bottom and the load was applied from the top with 
no further constraints, therefore allowing for the vertical displacement of the wall.  
 
Load application 
Both centric loading and eccentric loading (e = 55 mm) were simulated for crane and forklift model, 
by applying an axial load of 1200 kN and 1500 kN, respectively, with a static step on the upper 
loading cell (Fig. A 29). 
 
 
 
==PAGE:81==
 
66 
5.5.2 Results 
From the qualitative comparison of the stress distribution at the top surface of the HCSW for the 
two different models it can be seen, that the change of the connection type has a significant 
influence on the overall stress level and affects the load concentration at the edges of the section. 
The use of the forklift connection is unfavorable (See Fig. 75) for a uniform load distribution. 
 
 
Fig. 72 Comparison of van Mises stress at top of HCSW for crane and forklift connection 
When comparing centric and eccentric loading for the forklift model, it can be seen that in the 
eccentric case, the compressive stresses at the top of the HCSW concentrate at the former bottom 
of the slab (backside of the HCSW). The same behaviour but less pronounced could be seen for 
the crane model (Fig. 74). 
 
 
Fig. 73 Comparison of stress distribution on top section of forklift model for 1500 kN and 1200 kN (eccentric) 
==PAGE:82==
 
67 
 
Fig. 74 Comparison of stress distribution at top and bottom of HCSW for crane model with 1500 kN (centric) and 1200 kN (eccentric) 
Fig. 75 shows the transverse stress in the HCSW under an axial load of 1500 kN. The highest 
transverse stresses are to be found at the upper and lower part of the faces of the panel, stretching 
along the longitudinal axis of the element whereby the stress decreases. 
 
 
Fig. 75 Transverse stress distribution for axial load of 1500 kN for model with connection for crane (Left, Middle) and forklift (Right) 
==PAGE:83==

6 Full-scale tests of HCS-walls 
 
 
6 Full-scale tests of  
    HCS-walls 
==PAGE:84==
 
69 
6.1 
Test setup 
 
Three groups with three HCSW specimens each were tested under centric (Group C) and eccentric 
vertical loading (Group E20: 20 mm, Group E55: 55 mm). 20 mm were chosen as it equals the 
standard eccentricity used for wall calculations in Eurocode 2 and lies within the kern distance, 
indicating the limit for the cross section to remain under compression. This assumption neglects 
the effect of pre-tensioning. 55 mm, equivalent to a quarter of the cross-sectional height, was 
chosen to induce both compressive and tensile stresses across the section under loading.  
 
The load was applied displacement-controlled by an actuator at the bottom of the specimen. To 
ensure a conservative approach, the slabs were positioned so that the prestressing strands faced 
the back, preventing it from supporting any tensile forces that developed on the front face due to 
bending under eccentric loading (Fig. 76). A detailed drawing of machine and specimen as well as 
the step-by-step description of the testing program are given in the Appendix (p.192f.). 
 
 
 
Fig. 76 Testing set-up: Side and front view 
 
 
==PAGE:85==
 
70 
6.2 Specimens  
 
6.2.1 
Preparation 
 
Cutting 
Due to the limited capacity of the testing machine both with regards to the maximum applicable 
load and the available space for the specimen, the HCS had to be cut transversally to 3.2 m length 
and longitudinally in half to a width of approximately 600 mm. As the lateral sides of the specimen 
were uneven due to mortar leftovers, the middle of the slab was derived from the center of the 
prestressing steel strands. A string was span between the mid-points at each end to draw a central 
line onto the slab surface (Fig. 77). During the diamond-saw-cutting process, water is used to free 
the blade from dust. A clear coat was therefore applied to the lines drawn with a pen on the 
concrete surface to prevent them from being washed away. The cuts were clean and precise with a 
tolerance of max. 15 mm. Before and after cutting, the slabs were cleaned from dust. 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 77 Preparation of cutting lines (Left), HCS right before cutting (Middle) and cutting process with floor saw (Right) 
Application of speckle pattern for DIC 
The six specimens of groups E20 and E55 were painted in the front-facing middle part over an 
area measuring 1 m x 0.6 m. By gently pushing the nozzle of the spray can and holding it close to 
the concrete surface, the white base layer was then speckled with the maximum speckle diameter 
measuring ca. 1 mm (Fig. 78). 
 
 
 
Fig. 78 Preparation of white base layer (Left) and DIC pattern with close-up of speckles (Right) 
==PAGE:86==
 
71 
Top and bottom connection of the specimen 
The development of the connections at the end of the specimen had to consider limited 
dimensional capacities within the machine, the implementation of a locking system for different 
eccentricities, safety aspects during lifting and testing as well as compatibility with the available 
lifting gear. The originally planned top connection had to be re-designed as the operational cranes 
turned out to not reach the testing machine. Consequently, the specimens had to be positioned and 
after testing taken out of the machine with a forklift (Fig. 79). The connection consists of a steel 
U-profile for each end of the slab which are tied together with a threaded rod (Fig. 80 and Fig. 81). 
Easing erection and transport of the specimens, the use of a steel case also avoids drilling holes 
into the concrete matrix otherwise necessary for the mounting of lifting anchors. As the specimens 
showed dimensional variations, it was possible to mount the connections with a high precision 
before the erection of the element while the rigid steel profile allowed for an easier adjustment and 
exact positioning in the machine. Exact measurements of the connection are provided on p.192. 
The total volume of the top connection is 13’929’150 mm3 (ca. 0,01393 m3). Assuming a density 
of 78.5 kN/m³, an additional load of 1.09 kN is applied to the specimen. However, this load was 
neglected when determining the experimentally measured ultimate load. 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 79 Erected specimen hanging from crane (Left), forklift takes over for final positioning (Middle), transport after test (Right)  
 
Fig. 80 Bottom and top connection for specimens 
==PAGE:87==
 
72 
Assembly and erection of the specimen 
The bottom and top connection of the specimen were lifted with the crane onto each end of the 
slab and then secured by M24 bolts. Pieces of plywood between concrete and bolt helped to avoid 
the concentrated application of the clamping force. The edge distance from profile to concrete 
element was measured to be equal on opposing sides. After the correct positioning, M20 threaded 
bars were tightened to create a bracket for the specimen. After erection (Fig. 81), the HCSW was 
transported with the forklift to the testing machine and installed. 
 
 
 
Fig. 81 Mounting of the connection and erection of the specimen 
Positioning of the specimen in the machine 
To ensure for a correct placement of the specimen in the machine, for the centric as well as the 
eccentric tests, a locking mechanism was designed for both top and bottom connection (Fig. 82). 
 
 
Fig. 82 Locking mechanism for positioning of specimen 
==PAGE:88==
 
73 
The hinges were positioned centrically over the loading area with the steel cylinders of the machine 
as a reference (Fig. 83). The exact specimen position was ensured by the locking mechanism and 
timber wedges. No additional hinge had to be introduced for the top connection as the top of the 
machine already is a swivel head. The assumption of hinged conditions at both ends was verified 
by the deflection shape of the specimens tested under eccentric loading (p. 91). 
 
 
 
Fig. 83 Testing set-up (Left) and hinges at the bottom (Right) 
6.2.2 Geometrical properties 
The manufacturer of the hollow core slabs, Spæncom Aalborg, provided the original drawing of the 
hollow core slab for the donor building which was imported in Rhino for more exact measurements 
of the hollow cores and the geometry (See Fig. 84 and Fig. A 23 with more measures). 
 
Fig. 84 Cross section HCS PX22 with reinforcement details 
Table 11 shows an overview of the measured geometrical properties as well as the testing program 
of the specimens (Fig. 85). The total length of the hollow core slabs was in the range of 3.185-3.2 
m after cutting. The comparison of the width btop of PO_xA and PO_xB in Table 11, let assume  
that the longitudinal cuts were not straight, but the difference in the width actually results from 
filling mortar. The latter was partly still attached to the sides of the slab. This also explains why the 
sum of “PO_xA + PO_xB” differs from the design drawing (e.g. btop = 1157 mm, see Fig. 84). A 
string was spanned between the bottom ends of the HCS to measure the precamber at mid-length 
which ranged between 8-12 mm (See the measuring set-up in Fig. A 24).

6.2.3 Testing program 
Nine specimens were tested in total under vertical loading with three different load eccentricities. 
The loading scenario was randomly assigned. Fig. 85 illustrates the testing variants. 
 
Fig. 85 Schematic testing setup: Centric and eccentric testing (20 mm and 55 mm) 
==PAGE:90==
 
75 
6.3 Instrumentation 
 
6.3.1 
Manual control unit 
The machine works valve-controlled with a maximum vertical load of 10 MN and was operated by 
the same lab technician for all tests. A data logger (Fig. 86) was used for the data collection and 
monitored the vertical deformation of the machine as well as the resulting applied load on the 
specimen. The LVDTs were connected to the same data logger and channel for all tests to ensure 
a coherent data collection. The machine deformation was zeroed at tight contact between specimen 
and machine while the loading values were not changed for the documentation. 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 86 Machine with manual control unit before testing (Left) and during testing (Right) 
6.3.2 Linear variable differential transformers 
Linear variable differential transformers (LVDTs) are contact instruments that were used to 
measure the out-of-plane displacement along the back of the specimen. The aim was to determine 
the deflected shape of the HCSW during the tests and to gain a better understanding of the second-
order effects imparted to the wall, particularly at midspan. Limits evolve as a LVDT can only 
measure the displacement of a single point while damage to the instrument is possible when the 
concrete is close to failure. Differences in the lateral deformation along the width of the specimen 
were monitored as the measuring points were located in a straight, vertical row along the backside 
of the specimen. 
 
First, transversal metal profiles were mounted to the steel cylinders at the back of the machine. 
Then, each LVDT with a displacement capacity of 100 mm was connected to a magnetic arm and 
attached to the transversal metal profile. As the deflection shape of the element was of interest, the 
LVDTs were positioned at 0.4 m, 0.8 m, 1.6 m and 2.4 m (measured from the bottom of the 
specimen) in the middle of the slab width (Fig. 87 and Fig. 85). The end of each LVDT was 
extended with a steel pin that allows for vertical movement of the specimen without significantly 
affecting the results for the lateral deformation. Prior to the tests, the four LVDTs (LVDT 1-4, 
from top to bottom) were calibrated to convert the output in Millivolt to the corresponding value 
in Millimeter (Conversion is given in Table A 4).  
==PAGE:91==
 
76 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 87 Position of LVDTs (here: PO_2A) (Left), Close-up steel pin extension (Right) 
6.3.3 Video and photo documentation 
Photos of the specimen and testing set-up were taken during the preparation, positioning, before 
and after the testing. A screen was mounted in the field of view of the video camera showing in 
real time the applied load from the actuator in Millivolt, which corresponds to Kilonewton. For 
the centric tests, the video camera was positioned next to the manual control unit while the 
eccentric tests were filmed from the balcony in the testing hall (Fig. 86). Another camera (Canon 
EOS 850D) was placed in front of the specimens to cover the DIC area as well as the screen 
showing the applied force. Pictures were taken with a frame rate of ca. 0.25 pictures/sec. The 
camera settings were set to manual and the same for each of the eccentrically tested specimen (f6.3, 
1/640, ISO 600, focal length ca. 26 mm). Auto-focus and stabilizer were turned off. 
 
6.3.4 Digital Image Correlation 
Digital Image Correlation (DIC) is a non-contact photogrammetric measurement technique. Its 
advantage over single-point measurement techniques such as LVDTs is the acquisition of unlimited 
measuring points as the images are being automatically processed. DIC was used to analyze the 
deformation and strain distribution of the front face but also to detect cracks and their propagation. 
The field of view measured ca. 0.9 m x 1.35 m and the sensor of the camera has 4000 x 6000 pixel. 
To avoid an angled view, the camera was positioned on a table in front of the specimen with the 
objective at the height of the designated DIC area. A lamp was put at each side of the camera to 
uniformly illuminate the speckled part (Fig. 88). Before the test, the pattern quality was checked in 
Zeiss Correlate (Fig. 89). 
 
==PAGE:92==
 
77 
 
 
Fig. 88 Testing set-up with DIC (Left) and Field of View (FOV) of the camera (Right) 
 
Fig. 89 DIC – Check of pattern quality in Zeiss Correlate 
 
 
==PAGE:93==
 
78 
6.4 Results 
 
The failure mechanisms and ultimate loads varied among the full-scale specimens, but no 
significant outliers were observed, allowing all specimens to be included in the analysis. A detailed 
testing protocol for each specimen can be found in the Appendix (p. 210ff). 
 
6.4.1 
Comparability of the results 
 
Calibration of testing equipment 
 
• The machine was calibrated prior to the testing with a certified load cell and the testing 
procedure followed the same scheme for all specimens. The measurement of the applied 
deformation during the tests was consistent across all groups while the manual control of 
the machine lead to differences in the loading rate ranging approximately from 0.2-1 
mm/min (See Table A 5).  
• The LVDTs were calibrated before the first test to ensure accurate measurement of the 
deformations and were positioned at the same height for each specimen. 
 
The experimental results are facing the below stated limits with respect to the comparability of the 
specimens and the influence of the testing setup. 
 
Element-specific influences 
 
• Aiming for consistency: The five HCS originate from one production range of a precast 
company in Aalborg and were previously used at the same floor of an industrial building.  
• Diverse extent of mechanical damage to the elements around the lifting anchors 
• Partial mortar filling of hollow cores at the ends can increase the local capacity 
• The longitudinal straightness of the elements varies, possibly affecting the load-bearing 
capacity. 
• Diamond saw cutting: 
o Precision: Exact length (between 3.185-3.200 m, variation of 15 mm) and width of 
specimens differ. The latter also due to irregular mortar leftovers on the uncut 
longitudinal side. 
o Straightness of cuts: Cuts can be slightly skewed, resulting in a potential load 
concentration if the swivel head of the machine does not align properly to the 
surface. Alteration of the internal stress distribution is possible. 
o Homogeneity: Cutting the HCS into half might have weakened the specimens.  
• Exact precamber in erected position: The precamber was not measured after the erection 
of the specimen. Alterations are possible due to the reduction of the dead load in the 
normal direction of the slab. This can lead to an increase of precamber and consequently 
eccentricity which might reduce the global load-bearing capacity of the HCSW. 
 
Testing setup influences 
 
• The load is applied eccentrically for groups E20 and E55 but it is possible that the bottom 
hinges – due to their ring-shaped section – spread the load more uniformly onto the steel 
U-profile than intended, resulting ultimately in a lower actual eccentricity. 
• Variation in the testing rate: Manually, displacement-controlled, valve-driven test. 
According to Zhou et al., an increase of the displacement-controlled loading rate can restrict 
the development of micro cracks in hollow core panels which may lead to a temporary 
==PAGE:94==
 
79 
increment of stiffness and load-bearing capacity.(Zhou et al., 2023) The analysis of the 
experimental results, however, has shown no correlation between loading rate and ultimate 
load (See Fig. A 31). 
• No leveling layer (e.g. mesotite sheet) was used between specimen and steel connection 
possibly allowing for a more even load distribution. 
• The handling of the specimens was carried out by the same technicians for all tests. The 
exact positioning of the specimen inside the machine was challenging due to geometrical 
imperfections and the size of the specimens. Tolerances in the positioning can measure up 
to 5 mm which can influence the results.  
• Environmental factors such as variations in temperature or humidity can affect the material 
properties and cannot be entirely ruled out. The specimens were stored outside due to the 
limited space in the structural lab and brought into the testing hall 1-2 days before testing. 
Rainfall during the testing period may have caused variations in the humidity level of the 
specimens at the time of testing.

generally characterized by an initial steep 
section that is followed by a nearly linear part 
from ca. 200 kN until the ultimate load Nu is

Fig. 91 Load – deformation diagrams: Group C ( Left), Group E20 (Middle), Group E55 (Right) 
When comparing the load-deformation graphs per group, no coherent behaviour of the specimens 
depending on the load eccentricity can be detected (Fig. 91). The direct comparison of specimens 
originating from the same slab that were tested for different eccentricities (PO_1A/B and 
PO_4A/B) shows, that the initial vertical deformation grows with rising eccentricity of the load 
application, indicating a stiffer behaviour of the slabs tested under a lower load eccentricity. In 
contrast, it is similar for specimens from the same group, here PO_2A_C and PO_2B_C that were 
both tested under centric loading as well as PO_5A_E20 and PO_5B_E20 (Fig. 92). 
 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 92 Comparison of load-deformation diagrams of specimens originating from the same slab 
Only for the specimens PO_2B_C and PO_4A_E55, the ultimate load coincides with the global 
failure. For all other specimens, the deformation-driven test continued after reaching the peak load, 
determined by local damaging at the top or the splitting of the concrete body. The load is 
redistributed over the decreasing, remaining concrete section which is indicated by kinks of the 
load-deformation graph. This behaviour was especially pronounced for specimens PO_5B_E20, 
PO_3A_E55 and PO_1A_E55 (Fig. 93).

6.4.4 Cracking pattern 
 
Initial cracking load 
Considering all tested specimens, the longitudinal cracks initiated between ca. 968 kN and 1329 kN 
(Table 14). No difference was identified between the testing groups C, E20 and E55. An outlier is 
specimen PO_1A_E55 which showed a first crack at 448 kN.

Location and propagation of cracks 
The global cracking pattern of the specimens (Fig. 96) is characterized by longitudinal cracks. In 
most cases, they initiated in the upper part of the specimen and then propagated from underneath 
the flanges of the top connection slightly diagonally towards the narrowest part of the cross section 
(A1, A2, B, C as indicated in Fig. 95) from where they continued vertically downwards.

6.4.5 Failure mechanisms  
For some specimens, the ultimate load coincided with failure, while for others the redistribution 
of load following initial damage allowed continued loading before ultimate failure occurred (Table 
17). Global failure was induced by different mechanisms: Longitudinal splitting, Concrete 
crushing, Combination of splitting and concrete crushing or stability failure. Table 16 
presents a summary while examples are shown in Fig. 98.

Localized failures at top or bottom of the specimens – often leading to concrete cracking, spalling, 
and thereby reducing the effective loading area and load-bearing capacity – were common 
precursors to global failure. Load eccentricity played a role as it caused increased stress 
concentration at the bottom of the specimen, leading to localized damage (e.g. PO_4A_E55).

bottom part it was only detected for specimen PO_4A_E55. The prestressing strands were bent 
open after the surrounding concrete matrix had failed (Fig. 99).

Three specimens – one of each testing group and originating from three different slabs – failed

specimen. The split-off columns of PO_1B_C and PO_3A_E55 buckled in the transversal direction

6.4.6 Lateral deflection 
 
Note that the back of the specimen (bottom of the slab) is oriented to the left of the diagrams shown below and the 
front (top of the slab) to the right (See also Fig. 76). 
 
To show the progression of the lateral deformation of the specimens over the tests, the deflection 
figures at different load stages with the individual peak load as a reference are presented in Fig. 
105-Fig. 107. At 50 kN, it is assumed that proper contact has been established between specimen 
and U-profile of the connection as well as between connection and loading cell (See initial 
irregularities in Fig. 104 as an example) while 40% of the maximum vertical load marks the expected 
elastic limit of the material. The other stages (25% and 75% of Nu) complement the analysis of the 
lateral deflection. 
 
==PAGE:105==
 
90 
 
Fig. 104 Example for initial irregularities: Vertical load – Lateral deflection diagram until max. load for PO_3A_E55 
The absolute deflection of the centrically tested specimens reaches its maximum with 1.3 mm 
measured at a height of 2.4 m for PO_2A_C at the ultimate load (Fig. 105). PO_2A_C which had 
with 1348 kN the smallest failure load (and machine deformation) in its group, showed a deflected 
shape opposed to the direction of the precamber. All three specimens showed a similar deflection 
figure at 25% and 40% of the peak load (green and blue graphs in Fig. 105) while the increase was 
more pronounced from 40% to 75% of the ultimate load.  
 
 
Fig. 105 Lateral deflection at different load levels for specimen group C 
The specimens tested under eccentric loading (with e = 20 mm) show different deflection shapes 
(Fig. 106). The maximum deflection measures less than 1.5 mm at the peak load and is overall in 
the same range as for the centric tests. Specimen PO_4B_E20 is the only one showing a deflection 
figure similar to Euler case 2, suggesting hinged conditions at both ends. For the latter, the lateral 
deformation increased significantly from 75% of Nu to the ultimate load. The deflection shape of 
the other two specimens, PO_5A_E20 and PO_5B_E20, tends to a double curvature.

6.4.7 Digital Image correlation: Deformation and crack propagation 
The frame series of all eccentrically tested specimens were edited in Zeiss Correlate to analyze the 
strain in x-direction (horizontal) as well as the deformation in y-direction (vertical). As the 
displacement field corresponds to the stress field (M.P. Nielsen & L.C. Hoang, 2010), the DIC 
series allowed for the investigation of failure mechanism with a focus on the uniformity of the 
element compression. The lateral movement out-of-plane (< 1 mm for group E20, up to 4.4 mm 
for E55) was not considered in the post-processing. Fig. 108 illustrates the distribution of the 
vertical deformation over the DIC area at different load stages. It is noted that the actuator of the 
machine is located at the bottom of the specimen and moving upwards (y > 0) to compress the 
element. The images taken at the load stage closest to 50 kN, 0.25*Nu, 0.4*Nu, 0.75*Nu and Nu (as 
for the analysis of the lateral deformation) were chosen for the analysis.  
 
==PAGE:107==
 
92 
 
Fig. 108 Vertical deformation dy at different load stages (DIC area: 0.6 m x 1 m on front face of specimens) 
==PAGE:108==
 
93 
Not all specimens show a uniform deformation over the DIC area (0.6 m x 1 m) on the front face. 
While the frames of PO_4B_E20 are almost mono-colored, indicating a nearly perfect distribution 
of load and deformation over the investigated area, PO_3A_E55 shows from the beginning a 
skewed deformation distribution with the highest values in the upper left corner which correlates 
with the occurrence of the first cracks. The same behaviour, less pronounced, can be seen for 
PO_1A_E55. Specimens PO_5A_E20, PO_1A_E55 and PO_4A_E55 show a vertical 
deformation that is well-distributed in the horizontal direction but increases towards the top of the 
analyzed area. For PO_5B_E20 the inverse behaviour was observed. A table with the loads and 
image numbers at the different stages can be found in the Appendix (Table A 9). The vertical 
deformation as well as the horizontal strain were investigated in more detail to analyze the cracking 
and load (re-)distribution behaviour. 
 
Uniform load distribution 
For specimen PO_4A_E55, a uniform vertical deformation over the width of the cross section was 
observed, increasing towards the top of the specimen (Fig. 109): Point 1 measures a vertical 
deformation of 4.777 mm at 814 kN (62% of ultimate load), Point 2 and Point 3 4.151 mm and 
3.716 mm, respectively. The horizontal strain distribution aligns with the expected trajectories of a 
concrete body under compression.  
 
Fig. 109 PO_4A_E55: Vertical deformation and horizontal strain distribution at vertical load of 814 kN 
Abrupt cracking behaviour 
For PO_4B_E20, the horizontal strain (epsX) is equally distributed and close to zero over the 
studied DIC area right before the crack occurrence (Frame 71, Fig. 110). The diagram on the right 
shows an abrupt rise of the absolute horizontal strain (here rather the horizontal movement) of 
Points 1 and 2 with the crack opening (Compare frame 71 to 72). This indicates a brittle material 
behaviour. 
==PAGE:109==
 
94 
 
Fig. 110 Pre- and post-cracking analysis of PO_4B_E20 
The same was observed for specimens PO_1A_E55 and PO_5A_20 (Fig. A 52). Fig. 111 shows 
the diagrams of horizontal strain and vertical deformation over the duration of the test for three 
different points of specimen PO_1A_E55. The strain for Points 1-3 only rises as the crack appears 
whereby the magnitude of the rise corresponds to the crack width, decreasing from P1 to P3. The 
number of frames taken per second is not sufficient and the analysis not precise enough (See the 
oscillation of the graphs before the crack opening) to detected in detail at which horizontal strain 
the crack opened. 
 
 
Fig. 111 PO_1A_E55: Overview of DIC results 
Release of transverse tension after cracking 
Fig. 112 reveals that the vertical deformation of PO_3A_E55 is not perfectly uniform, but more 
pronounced in the upper left corner corresponding to the crack occurrence. The horizontal strain 
(epsX) in Point 1 increases prior to the crack opening, while it is released right afterwards as can be 
seen from the comparison of epsX_P1 (Frame 61) = 5.6% and epsX_P1 (Frame 71) = 0.33% (Fig. 
113). The release of the transverse strain after the crack opening was also observed for specimen 
PO_5B_E20 (Fig. A 53).

7 Discussion 
 
 
 
7 Discussion 
==PAGE:113==
 
98 
7.1 
Reusability of the elements 
 
7.1.1 
Condition 
It was found that the TSP phases (Transport, storage and processing) can have a significant impact 
on the structural integrity of reclaimed concrete elements. Removing the pouring and joint concrete 
to access the lifting anchors of the HCS for their deconstruction lead to mechanical damage. This 
negatively affects the reuse probability of the elements as the consideration of unique, local 
reductions of the cross-section is challenging for the structural design. The storage affects the 
durability of the elements as it accelerates the material deterioration: The ends of the prestressing 
strands that were exposed to the weather are corroded while the concrete surfaces experienced 
carbonation which lead to the reduction of the protective, alkaline layer over the reinforcement. A 
remaining concrete cover of ca. 30 mm over the reinforcement, was found to be sufficient for the 
HCS reuse. No major heterogeneities such as cracks, air bubbles or deformations were detected. 
Full-scale testing revealed that the damage in the anchor regions did not significantly affect the 
load-bearing behaviour of HCSW.   
 
Due to the visual assessment and given the relatively young age of the elements that were produced 
in 2006, it is concluded that the reuse of the elements is possible under the condition of appropriate 
repair of the anchor regions and the application of a protection layer onto the cut ends of the slabs 
to prevent further corrosion of the steel. Measures to improve the fire safety should be conducted 
according to project-specific requirements. 
 
Documentation of the storage conditions as well as the work conducted on reclaimed structural 
concrete elements from deconstruction to re-integration in a new building is essential for safe 
structural reuse. To ensure that no information is lost between the different actors that are handling 
the elements, labeling at the moment of deconstruction is proposed, with the possibility of adding 
information in a digital format. This could for example be a QR code leading to a digital product 
passport. 
 
7.1.2 
Material properties 
The material strength is decisive for the calculation of the remaining load-bearing capacity. It needs 
to be carefully investigated if the properties indicated in the historical structural calculations and 
the actual strength of the reclaimed elements coincide. The contact to the original producer of 
precast elements is recommended to acquire (testing) data of both concrete and reinforcement. It 
turned out to be especially important in the case of the reclaimed HCS as the material strength and 
distribution over the cross section was not listed in the structural calculations. The combination of 
concrete layers with different strengths as well as the geometrical layout posed a challenge to the 
strength assessment of the HCS. The thin top layer of concrete with could only be tested with a 
rebound hammer which resulted in rebound numbers similar for all slabs without significant 
outliers. The conversion of the rebound number to the average concrete strength lead to a result 
considerably inferior to the expectation (Concrete class C20/25 compared to fck = 45 MPa). 
Destructive testing of top and bottom layer was not possible due to cross-sectional limitations for 
the sample extraction. 
 
With respect to the main concrete matrix, the destructive tests faced the challenge of a limited core 
diameter and cube length compared to the requirements from the standard. Different core 
specimen heights resulted from the difficulty of precise diamond-saw-cutting during the 
preparation. The results are considerably inferior to the compressive strength communicated by 
the producer, with 30 MPa (Cores) and 27 MPa (Cubes) as mean value values. The characteristic 
strength of ca. 20 MPa for both, cores and cubes, is with ca. 43% considerably inferior to 35 MPa, 
the value provided by the supplier.  
==PAGE:114==
 
99 
The variety in the test results is explained with different sample sizes and shapes (cube, core) as 
well as heterogeneities of the concrete matrix (cracks, porosity, mortar leftovers) within the 
samples. In addition, the probably overpowerful testing machine with respect to the sample size 
can affect the strength results.  
 
It was shown that extensive, destructive material tests of a sufficient number of samples is essential 
to allow for a safe future implementation of reclaimed structural concrete elements. Given the 
deviation between testing results and information from the building archive and the producer, 
experimental values should create the basis for the calculation of the remaining load-bearing 
capacity of reclaimed structural concrete elements.  
 
7.1.3 
Additional testing 
Given the difference between strength values found in the static documentation and the results 
from the material tests, additional testing (methods) can increase the reliability of the results and 
help to determine the actual remaining strength of the HCS. For the (on-site) preassessment of the 
reuse potential of HCS it was found to be important to clarify the existence of any (non-)structural 
top layers on the HCS as the main structural material should be tested. This can pose challenges due 
to limited accessibility of the element when it is still integrated in an existing building. An additional 
testing of the prestressing steel is not deemed necessary as its contribution to the load-bearing 
capacity is neglected due to the assumed future use of the HCS as pure compression element. The 
determination of the exact remaining prestressing force which diminishes the axial load-bearing 
capacity would however enable a more accurate estimation of the remaining applicable vertical load 
on the wall. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:115==
 
100 
7.2 Discussion of full-scale tests 
The factors influencing the experimental results impose limitations on the conclusions and are 
detailed in section 6.4.1. 
 
7.2.1 
Cracking pattern 
 
Location of initial cracks and cracking load 
The observed cracking pattern, mostly longitudinal cracks, is in accordance with the behaviour of 
axially loaded HCP tested by (Zhou et al., 2023) (See p.25) who share the observation that the 
cracking pattern is influenced by the shape of the hollow cores. It was shown with analytical 
calculations, the FE model, the visual assessment of the cracking pattern as well as the DIC analysis 
of the full-scale HCSW tests that the location of the initial cracks depends on the local stress 
concentration. 
 
 
Fig. 116 Comparison of stress distribution over cross section in FE model and specimens PO_2A/B after axial loading tests 
The uniformity of the load distribution was mainly disturbed by the connection design which lead 
to stress concentration under the steel webs of the top connection, as proven by the cracking 
pattern at the end of the specimens and the comparison of two FE models with different 
connection types (Fig. 116). The video documentation has revealed that the cracks initiated in the 
upper part but not directly at the top of the slab which is explained with the strut-and-tie Model 2 
developed in section 5.2.2: The concrete splitting approximately started at the point where the two 
compression struts meet and the stress concentrates (Fig. 117). Both strut-and-tie models have 
skewed compression struts which have not only a vertical but also a horizontal component. On a 
cross-sectional level, the location of the cracks correlates with the thickness varying due to the 
hollow cores. The load is directed around the hollow cores. As it reaches the narrowest part of the 
cross section, the stress increases locally and exceeds the splitting strength (Fig. 117). 
 
==PAGE:116==
 
101 
  
 
Fig. 117 Superposition of cracking pattern and STM 2 for PO_5A_E20 and load distribution over cross section 
Analyzing all eccentrically tested specimens has shown that the load distribution was further 
affected by the partially skewed load application resulting from cutting tolerances and from the 
imperfect bond between connection and machine swivel head. The concentration of the vertical 
deformation (stress) correlates with the crack occurrence (Fig. 108). 
 
The results for the crack initiation load of the full-scale specimens (Table 14), ranging from 448 kN 
to 1329 kN, are in line with the calculated values for the maximum elastic load depending on the 
load application according to the theory of Schlaich and Schäfer as illustrated in Fig. 58. Assuming a 
as the load application width and b as the slab width, the analytical results range from 1549 kN (for 
a = b) to 426 kN (for a = b/2).  
 
From Schlaich and Schäfer’s theory, the experimental results and the DIC analysis, it can be 
concluded, that the load application width as well as the load distribution affect the initial cracking 
load with an even distribution over full width resulting in the highest value. 
 
==PAGE:117==
 
102 
Crack development: Longitudinal cracks 
The cracking pattern and propagation seen in the full-scale tests are similar to the findings from 
the experimental testing of hollow core panels by Zhou et al. (See 0): Local damage at the wall ends 
as well as single or multiple longitudinal cracks were detected. (E. Suenson, 1942) observed a variety 
of failure patterns for prisms under compression, of which the ones shown in Fig. 118 were found 
in the experimental tests of the HCSW.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 118 Failure patterns observed by (E. Suenson, 1942) for prism under compression, selected failure patterns of the HCSW specimens 
The longitudinal cracks and the splitting of the elements result from tensile stresses that occur 
perpendicular to the direction of loading due to the vertical movement of a conical part which 
causes the lateral displacement of the adjacent concrete as it tries to escape to the lateral sides. This 
behaviour could not only be seen for the front and back face of the HCSW but also at the lateral 
side (Fig. 119). The strain analysis of the eccentrically loaded specimens conducted in Zeiss Correlate 
has shown that only directly prior to the appearance of the crack, the concrete matrix would stretch 
horizontally. After the sudden crack opening, the strain was released and the load redistributed (See 
section 6.4.7). 
 
 
 
Fig. 119 Failure mechanism for rectangular block by concentrated strip load (M.P. Nielsen & L.C. Hoang, 2010) and upper part of 
specimen PO_4B_E20 
The occurrence and propagation of the longitudinal crack(s) on the front side was affected by the 
uniformity of the load application and the location of the hollow cores, more specifically the 
narrowest part of the cross section (See Fig. 95). Fig. 120 shows a conical fracture in the middle of 
the slab end of PO_1A_E55 as well as spalled-off corners. Still, the main longitudinal crack 
developed on the right side with another, smaller conical part at the top of it.  
==PAGE:118==
 
103 
(Lu et al., 2024) stated that the fracture behaviour, more specifically the crack location and 
development,  do not only depend on the external load (concentration) but are related to 
“composition, strength and the spatial distribution of its internal phases” which serves as an 
explanation for the observations during the full-scale tests.  
 
 
Fig. 120 Back side of PO_1A_E55 after testing: Concrete crushing and longitudinal crack 
Not all specimens were split over full height. Nielsen and Hoang claimed that in the case of a concrete 
cylinder, its height determines the extent of the crack. At sufficient height, the concrete is not split 
along full depth and the failure is only local. (M.P. Nielsen & L.C. Hoang, 2010) The full-scale 
experiments have shown both, local and global splitting. The crack propagation over the full 
specimen height happened suddenly. It can be assumed that with decreasing height of the HCSW, 
under the same loading and width conditions, full splitting of the specimen becomes more probable 
as a failure mechanism. With respect to the possible consideration of concrete splitting in analytical 
calculations, (Yue et al., 2024) have experimentally determined the splitting strength as 2.175 MPa 
for a concrete class C35/45 which is inferior to the mean tensile strength of 3.21 MPa indicated in 
Eurocode 2.  
 
Influence of hollow core geometry 
Splitting occurred at the narrowest parts (t = 30 mm) of the cross section. It is therefore concluded 
that the geometrical layout has an influence on the splitting behaviour, more specifically the 
minimum thickness of the cross section. As the hollow core geometry differs among producers 
and within their production lines, more research is necessary on the consideration of the minimum 
thickness on the cracking behaviour. 
 
To conclude, even for a similar testing setup with HCS of the same production line as a base 
material, the cracking pattern finally depends on local factors, that cover the geometrical layout of 
the HCS, material inhomogeneities but also the contact condition between load cell and specimen 
where any skewed application can lead to a local stress concentration affecting the crack initiation.  
 
==PAGE:119==
 
104 
7.2.2 Ultimate load and failure mode  
The experimental results give a conservative estimate of the load-bearing capacity of HCSW due 
to a partially skewed, imperfect loading area that was not further smoothened prior to the load 
application. A perfectly uniform load distribution was hindered by to the connection design. 
 
The analysis of the deformation distribution over the DIC area of groups E20 and E55 revealed 
that the uniformity of the deformation – and thus the load application, assuming a linear-elastic 
material behaviour (σc = Ec* εc) – affects the crack propagation and consequently the ultimate load. 
The peak load decreases with increasing  skew in the deformation pattern (Fig. 121). It is concluded 
that a clean, horizontal cut as well as an adequate load distribution can improve the load-bearing 
capacity. Deviations to the uniformity of the loading area, which can also result from the 
geometrical layout, e.g. a cut slab with a small edge area, are disadvantageous. 
 
Fig. 121 Deformation dy over DIC inspection area of group E55 just before peak load Nu 
It was further shown that load concentration can lead to local compression failure which affects 
the global limit state as the ultimate load decreases with every cross-sectional reduction by crushing 
or spalling. 
 
Both the analysis of the DIC series and the lateral deflection figures at different load stages for 
groups E20 and E55 indicated a change in the deformation behaviour of the specimens from 40 
to 75% of the peak load. Cracks started to appear and propagate while the increase of the lateral 
deformation was more pronounced probably resulting from the weakening of the specimen by 
cracking and local damages. 
 
The failure mode observed during the experiments is similar to the theory by Schlaich and Schäfer as 
the elements showed longitudinal cracks but the ultimate failure was induced by either the excess 
of the compressive strength or as the separated columns could not further take up the applied load 
and buckled (J. Schlaich & K. Schäfer, 2001). The cracking pattern was found to be in accordance 
with the studies on symmetrically reinforced hollow core concrete panels by (Zhou et al., 2023), 
suggesting that the one-sided reinforcement of the HCSW did not significantly alter the behaviour 
under axial loading. 
 
Effect of load eccentricity on peak load and failure mode 
The load eccentricity is likely to have an effect on the extent of the local damage as can be seen in 
Fig. 122. While the local damage of the specimens tested under centric loading is comparable at 
front and back side, it is different for PO_4B_E20 which was tested with a load eccentricity of 20 
mm. The front is less damaged than the back with the latter exhibiting concrete spalling that 
protruded into the hollow cores as well as freed and bent reinforcement. This observation is in 
accordance with the findings from the qualitative, numerical analysis (See Fig. 73). 
==PAGE:120==
 
105 
 
 
Fig. 122 Extent of damage: Comparison of front and back top part for two specimens of groups C and E20 
No correlation was found between maximum vertical load and load eccentricity when comparing 
the groups C and E20 (Fig. 123). None of the specimens of both groups showed a significant lateral 
deformation under loading suggesting the primary subjection to compressive stresses as expected 
from the kern distance. It can therefore be stated that bending did not significantly affect their 
ultimate load capacity. Lower ultimate loads for group E55 (Fig. 123) can be explained by a 
combination of bending and compression: The lateral deformation of the specimens resembles 
Euler case 2 and is more pronounced than for C and E20. In addition, the load eccentricity leads 
to a higher stress concentration at the edges of the specimens’ cross section. Overall, the 
geometrical and material layout of the elements supports the resistance to bending forces: High 
strength concrete (fck ≥ 45 MPa according to the supplier) at top and bottom of the cross section 
affects the moment of inertia, resulting in a higher bending resistance at the periphery of the cross 
section compared to a slab fully constituted of concrete with fck ≥ 35 MPa (Material of the main 
matrix). 
 
  
Fig. 123  Max. vertical load and corresponding machine deformation depending on eccentricity of load application

==PAGE:121==
 
106 
It is concluded, that in the case of group C and E20, the load eccentricity had a minor influence 
on the ultimate load as the specimens were mainly exposed to axial stresses. In contrast, Group 
E55, experienced lower peak loads due to the combination of compression and bending stresses. 
The load eccentricity had an effect on the lateral displacement curve but it did not ultimately 
provoke a global stability failure (out-of-plane buckling of the entire element). It is assumed that 
the concrete splitting strength was exceeded before the bending resistance as the latter is enhanced 
by high strength concrete layers at front and back face of the HCSW. 
 
Explanation of local stability failure 
The local stability failures were promoted by the previous splitting of the concrete matrix due to 
the (concentrated) vertical loading of the element. Once the concrete matrix is split, the lack of 
transversal reinforcement leads to stability problems. The concrete bodies are not connected to 
each other anymore, allowing for an outward-directed, in-plane and out-of-plane movement of the 
column that due to the reduced cross section has a lower buckling load than the original slab. This 
explanation is supported by the fact that stability failure was detected for three specimens 
originating from three different testing groups (C, E20 and E55).  
 
With regard to the previously conducted stability calculations in section 5.3, the following has been 
found: Assuming a uniform load distribution after the splitting of the HCSW specimen, the split-
off column would bear ca. 24% of the ultimate load measured during the experiments. In case of 
the three specimens with local stability failures, this results in ultimate column loads of 376 kN, 
384 kN and 242 kN for PO_1B_C (Nu = 1565 kN), PO_5A_E20 (Nu = 1600 kN) and PO_3A_E55 
(Nu = 1007 kN), respectively. All three values are below the theoretically determined ultimate 
concrete compressive strength of 648 kN and the calculated buckling load for the strong axis (2886 
kN). The Rankin load for the weak column axis, 301 kN, gives a conservative estimate of the failure 
loads of split-off columns of specimens PO_1B_C and PO_5A_E20.  
 
Influence of the cracking pattern on the ultimate load 
(J. Schlaich & K. Schäfer, 2001) stated that the reduction of the compressive strength depends on 
the direction of the cracks. It is small if the cracks follow the direction of the load and the concrete 
bodies between the cracks can be considered prismatic columns while it is bigger for skewed cracks, 
especially if two sets of intersecting cracks occur which was detected for PO_3A_E55 (Fig. 124) 
and explains the lowest failure load of all tested specimens (Nu = 1007 kN).  
 
 
 
Fig. 124 Intersecting, skewed cracks: PO_3A_E55 
==PAGE:122==
 
107 
The other two specimens tested with a load eccentricity of 55 mm did not show longitudinal cracks 
to a similar extent as groups C and E20 which is explained with their earlier failure due to the 
combination of compression and bending before the splitting could occur. 
 
7.2.3 Calculation of remaining load-bearing capacity with strength values from testing 
If the characteristic value for the concrete compressive strength of the HCS (35 MPa as a 
conservative assumption, neglecting the parts with fck = 45 MPa) is assumed which was provided 
by the original  producer Spæncom, a ultimate axial load-bearing capacity of 2735 kN is the 
theoretical result. When applying the partial safety factors for concrete and the coefficient that 
takes unfavourable effects resulting from the load application into account, as well as considering 
the factor 1.35 for knowledge level III (limited) according to Eurocode 8-III, a design axial load Nu 
of 807 kN (1148 kN without PT force) is calculated:

7.2.4 Determining the ultimate load under the influence of splitting (cracks) 
Schlaich and Schäfer state that transverse stresses resulting from the stress field in walls under axial 
compression theoretically reduce the capacity of unreinforced concrete to simultaneously absorb 
compressive stresses (See p. 53). The multiaxial stress would however not destroy the concrete 
matrix unless the compressive stresses exceed 80% of the uniaxial strength of the concrete (Here: 
fck,lim = fck*0.8 = 35 MPa * 0.8 = 28 MPa) while cracks would form due to the tensile stress which 
leads to the separation of the element. As a consequence, the tensile stresses are released. In 
principle, the uniaxial strength in the concrete bodies between the cracks is then available again. 
Due to the irregular crack propagation, the average accountable compressive stress is reduced 
compared to the uncracked concrete element.  
 
Lateral deformation and effect of precamber and prestressing force 
In order to study the effects of the lateral deflection at mid-span on the specimen behaviour, the 
theoretical stresses were calculated for group E55 whose specimens have all shown a deflection 
figure similar to Euler case 2. A second iteration was carried out to consider the pre-tensioning 
force (in addition to the value of Nu) as well as a precamber of 10 mm (in addition to the load 
eccentricity). The stress at front and back face at mid-height of the slab was calculated as follows:

of 35 MPa (conservative assumption) with 36 MPa. This suggests that pretensiong force (and 
precamber) have an influence on the axial load-bearing capacity. If they are considered in the 
calculations, the specimens exceed the theoretical compressive strength by 15-42% (assuming 
C35/45) while the front face experiences with 5.8-7.4 MPa tensile stresses at mid-height that are 
considerably higher than the theoretical capacity of both C35/45 and C45/55.

109 
It could not be determined with certainty, how precamber and prestressing force influenced failure 
mechanism and ultimate load of the HCSW. The bond between concrete and steel was found to 
be good as the matrix stayed intact until the point of failure (concrete crushing). It can therefore 
be supposed that the strands did not locally weaken the cross section to the extent that splitting 
would occur in their proximity. Local failure occurred to the same extent at both sides, suggesting 
a uniform prestressing of the section. A local reduction of the compressive stress capacity of the 
former bottom side of the slab (back side of the wall specimens) by the prestressing force of the 
strands was not detected. The width of the longitudinal cracks were bigger at the front face which 
can result as a combination of both prestressing and load eccentricity.  
 
Looking at all nine specimens, the consideration of the pre-tensioning force leads to an increase of 
the peak load by 21-34%, with 1600 kN and 1007 kN as maximum and minimum values from the 
experiments. The average maximum compressive stress results then as 17.8-24.8 MPa (12.9-20.5% 
without PT force), assuming an idealized, undamaged cross section. Given the considerable value 
of the PT force compared to the ultimate load measured in the experiments, it is recommended to 
reduce the ultimate load capacity of the HCSW by the prestressing force indicated by the producer. 
 
Possible alterations to the extent of the precamber due to the vertical erection of the slabs need to 
be further investigated.  
 
Possible alterations to the testing setup and their effect 
• Rotating the concrete slab 180 degrees around its longitudinal axis, thus placing the 
prestressing strands on the front face, is unlikely to significantly alter the failure mechanism 
or ultimate load, as the slab's bending under eccentric load and the resulting tensile stresses 
(then absorbed by the reinforcement) were not critical factors in either the failure or the 
ultimate load observed during the experimental tests. 
• The use of detachable U-profiles for the fork lift transport at the top can lead to a more 
uniformly distributed load application over the cross section. 
• DIC pattern applied onto the entire specimen front face to detect the occurrence of the 
first crack and the overall crack propagation more precisely for both centric and eccentric 
tests as well as allowing for the correlation of stress distribution and failure mechanism. 
• Use of a laser mounted to the upper load cell and directed towards the U-profile that 
supports the HCSW to measure the absolute vertical deformation of the specimen and 
exclude the influence on the results by the machine’s steel cylinders’ deformation. 
• Measuring the precamber of the vertically erected specimen prior to the testing. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:125==
 
110 
7.3 Comparison of analytical investigation and experimental results 
 
In the following, the agreement of analytical and experimental results is assessed and the suitability 
of the various theories discussed. 
 
Limitations of the comparison 
The mixture of concrete layers with different strengths (fck = 35 MPa and fck = 45 MPa) was not 
considered in the analytical investigations to limit the complexity of the calculations. On the safe 
side, a characteristic compressive strength of 35 MPa, a Young’s modulus of 34 GPa and a mean 
concrete tensile strength of 3.21 MPa were assumed for the entire section. This affects the 
comparability with the experimental results. Uncertainties remain about the actual concrete 
strength values as the results from the material tests are considerably inferior to the manufacturer’s 
information and the tests could not be carried out according to existing standards with the 
maximum core diameter being restricted by the geometry of the HCS section.  
 
7.3.1 
Overview experimental and analytical results 
Fig. 125 presents an overview of the experimental and analytical results while the following sections 
discuss the findings in more detail. 
 
Fig. 125 Overview of experimental and analytical results

between the four different Rankin load scenarios: From the pure consideration of the load

and prestressing force (orange, horizontal dash-dot line). The ultimate load was calculated on the 
safe side for all specimens of group E55 when considering a precamber of 10 mm in the Rankin 
method. When neglecting the effect from the load eccentricity as well as the pretensioning force

The Rankin load (continuous, horizontal lines), calculated without considering precamber and 
pretensioning force, gives a conservative estimation of the ultimate load (vertical lines) of all three 
groups except for specimen PO_2A_C. For groups E20 and E55, the Rankin load estimates the 
peak load rather conservatively, as it is exceeded by 26% for the specimen with the lowest ultimate 
load of group E20 (Nu, min = 1438 kN) and 44% for group E55 (Nu,min = 1007 kN), respectively. 
The additional consideration of both precamber and pretensioning leads to values considerably 
below the experimentally measured capacity. With respect to the ultimate compressive strength 
and assuming a concrete of class C20/25, 64-102% of the cross section were activated. 
 
To conclude, the Rankin method is a conservative approach to calculate the HCSW peak load for 
all loading scenarios when taking into account either precamber or pretensioning and using the 
strength values from destructive testing.

7.3.3 Mohr-Coulomb theory 
The Mohr-Coulomb theory, assessing the load-bearing capacity of concrete in the plastic state, 
neglects any eccentricity in the load application. It gives a upper bound solution for the ultimate 
failure load of the specimens. A maximum compressive stress of 26.61 MPa and 30.22 MPa were 
calculated considering different values for cohesion and friction angle in the literature. If a uniform 
load distribution over the entire cross section of the HCSW is assumed, this leads to failure loads 
of 2079 kN and 2362 kN, respectively, overestimating the maximum measured vertical load of 
1600 kN (Specimen PO_5A_E20) by 23% and 32%. The deviation between analytical and 
experimental results can be explained with a reduced activated cross section. A limited suitability 
can also result from the fact that the theory is developed for massive, rectangular concrete block 
while the specimens have a complex cross-sectional layout including 3.5 hollow cores. 
 
7.3.4 Theory of Schlaich and Schäfer: Strut-and-tie models, cracking and ultimate load 
The two STM (Model 1 and 2) that were developed supported the explanation of the cracking 
pattern as well as the load distribution in the undamaged and damaged state (after spalling of 
corners at the top of the specimen) of the HCSW. As shown previously, the initial cracking load 
of the specimens depends on the extent of the cross-sectional activation and can be determined 
with the theory developed by Schlaich and Schäfer. The experimental tests have shown that the 
ultimate applicable load is inferior in the cracked state as the continuation of the test (increasing 
the vertical deformation) lead to an increasing damage level fostering the further decrease of the 
load-bearing capacity (See Fig. 93). According to Schlaich and Schäfer, this can be considered by 
applying a reduction factor of 0.7 to the design compressive strength in the uncracked state (fcd).  
 
The comparison of ultimate load Nu to the maximum applicable load after reaching limit state, 
shows that apart from specimen PO_1B_C, the reduction factor proposed by Schlaich and Schäfer 
gives a conservative assumption of the remaining load-bearing capacity after the cracking of the 
specimens with the ratio Nu,II/Nu spanning from 0,88 to 0,97 (Table 19).
