7.3.5 Possible strengthening measures for HCSW 
(Zhou et al., 2023) reported that confining frames, consisting of a RC ring beam and tie columns, 
were able to significantly increase the structural ductility of hollow core panels subjected to axial 
and in-plane as well as out-of-plane lateral loads. They further prevented the hollow core units 
from collapse and helped to maintain a considerable post-cracking load-bearing capacity. It would 
also limit the crack opening and thus prevent any local stability failure as seen in the full-scale 
experiments of the HCSW. The concept of confined load-bearing panels has already been proven 
technically feasible for structural reuse elements as shown in section 2.1.3 and should be further 
explored for the case of HCSW. 
 
 
==PAGE:129==
 
114 
Integrating HCSW through the use of unbonded post-tensioning cables would allow for a building 
design according to Design for Disassembly and Deconstruction (DfDR) principles and support 
the overall integrity of the panels as (Zhou et al., 2023) found in experimental tests of HCP but 
reduce the vertical load-bearing capacity.  
 
Schlaich and Schäfer state that for load application widths a bigger than 80% of the height H of the 
wall, a parallel compressive stress field results requiring little constructive transverse reinforcement 
as no significant tensile stresses occur. In the case of HCSW, this would already be the case for the 
positioning of two slabs next to each other (a = 2 * 1.2 m = 2.4 m ≈ 0.8 * 3.2 m) assuming an 
appropriate bond between the slabs. 
 
Mounting multiple HCSW in a row and subjecting them to concentrated loading, results in lower 
transverse tensile forces in the center of the elements but leads to additional transverse tensile 
forces T1 at the edge (Fig. 128) (J. Schlaich & K. Schäfer, 2001). The latter can be accounted for 
by a confinement at the ends with steel or reinforced concrete beams and columns. The 
confinement approach has already been shown in practice (Fig. 6). 
 
Fig. 128 Adjacent compression fields: Support in the transverse direction (J. Schlaich & K. Schäfer, 2001) 
 
==PAGE:130==
 
115 
7.4 ReHoCS - Reusing Hollow Core Slabs: A modular construction system 
 
 
Fig. 129 AI-generated house from HCSW 
7.4.1 
Concept 
A conceptual design (Fig. 130) is proposed for a single-story building made from reclaimed hollow 
core slabs that are acting as vertical, horizontal, and pitched structural elements. The reuse elements 
are intended for interior use and need to be protected from the weather. Potential dimensional 
tolerances are accounted for with a mortar layer which also provides the element at its cut ends at 
top and bottom with protection from fire and deterioration. This is especially important with 
respect to any exposed reinforcement. As the lateral load-bearing capacity of the HCSW has not 
yet been verified, an additional stiffening system needs to be introduced, which in this context is a 
confining frame of RC columns and beams. The fire safety of the building should further be 
ensured by protecting layers. 
 
The goals of the design were derived from the literature research and follow the principles of 
modularity, adaptability and reversibility: 
 
1. Maximize the (re)use potential through flexibility in structural typology and modularity. 
2. Reduce the dependency on geometrically unique elements through modular cut-outs. 
3. Ensure ease of assembly and disassembly with standard components (elements and 
connections). 
 
Modularity is given by the use of standardized components, HCS, with a standard width of 1.2 m 
establishing a raster with the same distance. Depending on available span lengths of the reclaimed 
elements, the wall height can be adjusted and the roof be pitched. The connection between the 
elements can be made from low-strength mortar to ensure sufficient insulation while allowing for 
an easy future disassembly. Key features of the ReHoCS system include prefabrication, modularity 
and operational advantages such as fast construction time. This concept allows for waste reduction, 
minimizing the CO2 impact, while supporting disassembly and relocating the panels. 
 
Fig. 130 shows an exemplary stock of reclaimed hollow core slabs (grey) as well as confining 
elements (colored), while in Fig. 131 these elements are assembled to a single-storey house. 
==PAGE:131==
 
116 
 
 
Fig. 130 Stock of hollow core slabs (grey) and additional confining elements (colored) for one ReHoCS house 
 
Fig. 131 Assembly process ReHoCS house 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:132==
 
117 
7.4.2 Example calculation for residential building  
The specimen PO_3A_E55 showed the lowest ultimate load with 1007 kN which was applied with an eccentricity of 55 mm. Using 
1000 kN as the lower bound for the HCSW with a width of 600 mm (conservative assumption), this would result in a ultimate, normalized 
line load of ca. 1.67 MN/m or 2000 kN for an entire, uncut HCSW with a standardized width of 1.2 m. The equivalent, normalized 
line loads for all specimens are shown in  
Table A 7. With the presumption to not exceed the elastic limit, the minimum crack initiation load, 
448 kN for specimen PO_1A_E55, is taken as a reference resulting in a normalized line load of 
746 kN/m. 
 
To assess the reuse potential of HCSW, an example calculation for the wall line load on the ground 
floor of a 5-storey residential building was carried out. The HCSW do not form part of the 
stabilizing system. A standard building layout with floor spans of 6 m and a room height of 3 m 
was assumed. Wall 1 (See Fig. 132) is decisive and supports the ends of two floor slabs with a total 
load draw-in width of 6 m. The floor slabs are assumed to be massive and cast-in-place 
(conservative assumption) with a thickness of 25 cm. The walls were considered to be HCSW, the 
additional dead load was taken as 5 kN/m2 on the roof, on the other floors 2.5 kN/m2 while the 
imposed load was assumed as 5 kN/m2 for the roof (incl. building services equipment) and as 
2.7 kN/m2 for the other floors (incl. partition walls).  
 
The result was rounded up on the safe side 
and results in a wall line load of ca. 500 
kN/m. This is less than a third of the 
ultimate loading capacity of the HCSW 
with the lowest result measured during the 
experiments (1670 kN/m) and 67% of the 
minimum elastic limit (746 kN/m) that 
covers a load eccentricity of 55 mm. With 
respect to the axial load capacity, an 
implementation of HCSW in residential 
buildings with a limited number of storys 
(here shown for 5 storys) is considered 
feasible.  
 
The detailed calculations can be found in the 
Appendix p. 227. 
 
Fig. 133 shows that for the upper floors the 
bending moment can be decisive for the 
structural design of the HCSW while for 
the floors closer to the ground level, the 
vertical load increases and the effective 
load eccentricity from the floor slab load 
decreases. 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 132 Exemplary residential building 
==PAGE:133==
 
118 
 
Fig. 133 Load eccentricity on HCSW 
The detail shown in Fig. 133 is a conservative assumption. To minimize the moment, a continuous 
slab, supported on top of the wall section, can be applied and the HCSW positioned in a manner, 
that benefits from the existing precamber.

8 Conclusion and future considerations 
 
 
 
I like change. It’s not scary to me. We have to 
realize that circularity will soon become part 
of the standard.” 
 
Gilli Hobbs, Keynote speaker Circular Build Forum ‘25  
Conclusions  
& 
Future Work 
==PAGE:135==
 
120 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
8.1 
Conclusions 
 
The study examined the technical feasibility of repurposing used concrete hollow core slabs (HCS) 
as vertical load-bearing elements. The option to reuse reclaimed HCS as walls or slabs, depending 
on the properties of the available stock and the requirements from the new building, facilitates 
more versatile structural reuse and enhances the financial feasibility.  
 
A comprehensive literature review identified general challenges for structural concrete reuse that 
impact both process and structural design. An experimental proof of concept was conducted using 
reclaimed HCS sourced from a former industrial building in Aarhus. To accommodate the testing 
machine, the HCS were longitudinally cut in half and – after their vertical erection – subjected to 
axial loading until failure. Three different load eccentricities – 0, 20 and 55 mm – were investigated, 
with three specimens for each group.  
 
The central research questions were answered as follows: 
 
1. What is the failure mechanism and axial load-bearing capacity of used concrete hollow core 
slabs that are repurposed as walls? 
 
Longitudinal cracking occurred on the front and lateral sides of the specimens. The hollow core 
geometry influenced the cracking behaviour, resulting in crack development at the narrowest parts 
of the cross section. It was shown that the uniformity of the load distribution affects the crack 
initiation load and location, as well as the ultimate load. The latter is further influenced by the 
direction of cracks with diagonal and intersecting cracks leading to earlier failure. No global stability 
failure was observed, however, for one specimen from each testing group, the longitudinal cracks 
led to the full splitting of the concrete body. This was followed by the buckling failure of a split-
off column, out-of-plane in two cases and transversely in one case. Splitting and buckling were 
exacerbated by the absence of transverse reinforcement in the HCS.  Concrete crushing, primarily 
occurring at the upper corners of the specimens, reduced the loading area, which fostered a more 
concentrated load application and contributed to the global brittle failure of the elements. Both 
splitting and concrete crushing led to the redistribution of the load.  
 
For groups C and E20, the load eccentricity did not appear to influence the ultimate load, which 
ranged from 1348 to 1600 kN. This is likely attributed to the material distribution, with high 
strength concrete at the edges of the HCS cross section, while the eccentricity of 20 mm remained 
within the core radius, allowing for full compression of the cross section.  
Highlights: 
 
• 
Wall reuse is challenged by dimensional limitations and the often building-specific reinforcement layout. 
• 
The lack of a standardized, structural design concept for reclaimed concrete elements was identified. 
• 
Destructive material tests of core and cube specimens from reclaimed HCS resulted in a characteristic 
concrete compressive strength significantly inferior to strength values provided by the original supplier. 
• 
The testing of vertically erected hollow core slabs under axial loading showed the importance of a uniform 
load distribution over the cross section for the ultimate load-bearing capacity.  
• 
The hollow core geometry, especially the minimum depth of the cross section, influence crack location 
and propagation. 
• 
The global, brittle failure occurred due to cross-sectional reductions following the local excess of the 
concrete compressive strength in the connection areas but also due to longitudinal splitting of the 
concrete matrix and the insufficient capacity to redistribute the load. 
• 
Using knowledge level factors inspired by EC8-III for the analytical calculation of the ultimate load gives 
a conservative estimate of the ultimate load-bearing capacity depending on the level of testing and 
available documentation. 
• 
The potential of a modular concept for concrete hollow core slabs being reused as horizontal and vertical 
elements was shown. 
==PAGE:136==
 
121 
In contrast, Group E55 exhibited a more pronounced lateral deformation compared to the other 
two groups with similar deflection figures for the three specimens. This group experienced earlier 
failure due to the combined effects of compression and bending with peak loads spanning from 
1007 to 1310 kN.  
 
2. Which analytical methods are suitable to estimate the remaining load-bearing capacity of 
hollow core slab walls (HCSW)?  
 
The remaining load-bearing capacity strongly depends on the material strength. Information from 
the building archive alone cannot replace the thorough investigation of the structural elements 
through comprehensive visual assessment and destructive material testing. The properties that 
require testing may vary based on the intended future use of the element – whether it is subjected 
to compression, tension, and/or bending. For HCSW, if they are utilized solely as compression 
elements, determining the characteristic concrete compressive strength is a fundamental basis for 
applying any analytical method. 
 
To conservatively assess the reuse potential and case-specific suitability of reclaimed concrete 
elements at various project stages, it is recommended to use knowledge level factors comparable 
to those outlined in EC 8-III for the structural design. Depending on the availability of historical 
and pre-demolition documentation, as well as test results, a safe estimate of the remaining load-
bearing capacity can be made. For the HCSW, it has been demonstrated that by incorporating these 
knowledge level factors into the calculation of the ultimate load and further reducing this value by the 
pre-tensioning force, a conservative estimate of the axial load-bearing capacity can be achieved in 
relation to the experimental results. Load eccentricities that exceed the standard values for walls 
(20 mm) should be taken into account.  
 
Concrete splitting and localized excess of the compressive strength are critical factors influencing 
the ultimate load and failure mode of HCSW. As a lower bound solution, the analytical method 
developed by Schlaich and Schäfer for the calculation of the initial cracking load has proven suitable 
for the investigated slabs. This method allows for the consideration of the activated cross-sectional 
area under axial loading, which is particularly important for the reuse of structural concrete 
elements. Due to the cutting process, these elements often exhibit greater dimensional tolerances 
than conventional precast components, potentially disrupting uniform load distribution. 
 
If the knowledge level factors are applied to the material strength, the Rankin method results in a 
conservative estimate of the ultimate vertical load on HCSW, taking into account the influence of 
load eccentricity. In any case, the ultimate load should be reduced by the pre-tensioning force acting 
on the HCS. 
 
3. How does the one-sided pre-tensioning of hollow core slabs influence their axial load-
bearing capacity when repurposed as walls? 
 
No differences in the cracking pattern were observed when comparing the experimental results to 
those found in the literature regarding symmetrically reinforced hollow core panels subjected to 
axial loading. As previously mentioned, the pre-tensioning force should be considered in the 
calculations of the load-bearing capacity, while the role of the precamber requires further 
investigation, including potential alterations resulting from vertical erection.  
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:137==
 
122 
4. How can HCSW be integrated into new buildings, and what limitations exist? 
 
ReHoCS system: Unlocking future potential through simple structural layout, modularity and DfD connections  
A system has been designed that repurposes HCS as both walls and floor slabs within a single 
structure. The walls are intended to be simply supported as pure compression elements. This 
straightforward structural layout enhances the likelihood of future reuse and eliminates the need 
for additional reinforcement to accommodate moments at the top and bottom of the wall. The 
advantages of this proposal include modularity in construction, the potential for prefabrication, 
and the easy replacement of damaged modules in the subsequent life cycle stage. However, 
challenges may arise during the processing of reclaimed concrete and in ensuring fire safety. 
 
Importance of a uniform load distribution 
A uniform load distribution positively affects the load-bearing capacity of HCSW. This underscores 
the importance of the processing stage, particularly the precision of cuts and the connections, 
which must accommodate for dimensional tolerances without compromising on the uniformity of 
the load distribution. To prevent load peaks, HCSW with cut ends should not be positioned at the 
ends of an element row.  
 
Avoiding transversal tensile stresses in the elements 
The HCSW should be laterally restrained using either reinforced concrete or steel elements.  
 
Fire safety and sound insulation 
Depending on the project and wall construction, fire safety and sound insulation may pose 
limitations on the use of HCSW in buildings. Localized damage compromise fire safety and should 
be repaired accordingly. 
 
8.2 General learnings for structural concrete reuse 
 
Comprehensive information about material strength is essential for ensuring the safety of structural reuse 
Significant discrepancies have been observed between the expected and experimentally measured 
concrete strength values. This highlights the importance of collecting thorough material data from 
building archives, manufacturers, and through both non-destructive and destructive testing, as 
strength values directly influence the calculated load-bearing capacity. The primary focus should 
be on improving the accuracy of material strength assessments to ensure maximum reliability. 
 
Connection design significantly impacts the load-bearing capacity of reused structural elements 
It was demonstrated through the analysis of a DIC area that the uniformity of the load distribution 
significantly influences both cracking behaviour and ultimate load capacity. The importance of 
clean cuts and appropriate connection design for reclaimed structural elements is emphasized. 
Forces are imposed on the structural element during disassembly and relocation processes, which 
should be taken into account, as they can impact material robustness at both the micro (cracks) 
and macro level (concrete spalling).  
 
Full-scale tests showed the significance of processing and preparing the elements 
Deviations from the uniformity of the loading area negatively impact the ultimate bearing capacity. 
 
Efficient handling of reclaimed concrete elements 
During the handling and erection of the elements for testing, the question arose regarding the best 
practices for managing these elements. Specifically, how can the workload be minimized while 
maximizing the potential for future use. 
 
==PAGE:138==
 
123 
8.3 Call for action 
 
More testing of existing structures is essential to better understand the reliability of the information contained in the 
building archive 
Deviations from plans and execution should not to be underestimated, as they can concern both 
concrete quality and reinforcement layout. 
 
Different approaches exist to assess the remaining structural capacity of used concrete elements, however, none of these 
are standardized. Simple calculation methods are necessary to facilitate structural concrete reuse. 
Currently, the structural capacity is determined through a range of methods, from a basic evaluation 
of material information found in the building archive to extensive testing at both the material and 
element scale. Safety margins for the load-bearing capacity are established individually by the 
responsible engineer, based on an analysis of available documentation and testing data, or by 
applying safety factors specified in standards for new (precast) concrete elements.  
 
Analytical methods that provide a conservative estimate of the load-bearing capacity of structural 
elements present an opportunity for the broader implementation of structural reuse. Further 
research is needed to explore how the unique characteristics of reclaimed concrete elements, 
including variations in geometry and damage, can be accounted for. For example, this can be 
achieved through appropriate safety factors, as the FE analysis of dimensionally diverse elements 
is not always practical. 
 
Standardized verification schemes for the reuse of structural concrete elements are essential to promote their safe 
reintegration in new buildings 
Standards help to scale the implementation of reclaimed elements by minimizing the individual risk 
of building developers and engineers who may be held accountable for issues related to strength 
and durability. However, the standardization is challenged by the unique condition of concrete 
elements, which can vary based on their diverse origin (such as exposure, consequence class, pre-
loading), possible damage incurred during deconstruction and transport, environmental conditions 
during storage, and the proper design of joints for reintegration. Therefore, comprehensive and 
standardized guidelines for quality documentation of reused elements throughout the process are 
essential. 
 
8.4 Future research  
 
Material testing 
The impact of the specimen size on the outcomes of destructive compression and flexural strength 
tests warrants further investigation as does the number of samples required to ensure the 
robustness of the testing data and minimize the influence of outliers on characteristic strength 
values. Given the cross-sectional limitations of HCS, it is essential to explore how the testing data 
from cylindrical cores with a limited diameter can be correlated with those that meet standard 
diameter specifications. Additionally, further research is needed to assess the effect of concrete age 
on the results of core and cube compression tests. When determining the compressive strength 
experimentally, the question arises how other material properties, such as Young’s modulus and 
tensile strength, can be adjusted if the test results deviate from the historical documentation. In 
this context, the compression tests indicated a characteristic strength of 20 MPa and the material 
properties were conservatively adopted in accordance with Eurocode 2, assuming a concrete class of 
C20/25. Another central question is how the results of experimental testing on reclaimed material 
can be utilized. Conducting tests with second-hand elements means testing material whose 
structural qualities will differ depending on their origin, prior use and exposure conditions.  
 
 
==PAGE:139==
 
124 
Explaining the complex behaviour of HCS under axial load depending on hollow core geometry and prestressing 
layout 
The behaviour of HCSW under load is influenced by an advanced cross-sectional geometry, 
prestressing, and eccentricities in load application. As a continuation of this work, a parametric FE 
model incorporating a concrete plasticity model can be created to account for these factors.  
 
Role of aspect ratio of HCSW 
The aspect – height-to-length – ratio conditions the behaviour HCSW which requires further 
investigation. 
 
Study on behaviour under lateral loading 
It is proposed to also assess the shear capacity of the HCSW to enhance their flexibility for future 
applications. 
 
Consideration of durability and creep 
Long-term studies are essential to determine the durability of structures constituted of reclaimed 
concrete elements. What is a realistic reuse limit with regard to material degradation? How many 
life cycles can be expected for concrete elements? Further, the effect of creep on the element 
behaviour after a typology change is yet to be studied. 
 
Investigation of commercialization potential 
The theoretical stock of HCS should be mapped to estimate the availability of the resource as well 
as the typical dimensions of the elements, while also the distribution of span lengths within the 
stock is of great interest. This allows for an efficient reintegration of reclaimed concrete elements 
into new buildings, considering the most commonly required room heights and span lengths today.  
 
Development of DfD connections for structural reuse 
Further studies on (dry) joints that facilitate easy disassembly are necessary to address the challenges 
of structural reuse, including the ability to level the contact surface of the reclaimed elements. 
 
Without tackling the systemic challenges, such as improving the legal framework for structural 
reuse, developing financially viable models for logistics and incentivizing circular construction for 
both the supply and demand sides, the potential for scaling structural reuse remains limited.

==PAGE:153==
 
138 
A Interviews  
 
All interviews were recorded and transcribed. 
 
Interview with Hanna Walter (Insenti AS Norway) 
17.09.2024 via Teams 
 
Participants 
 
• Hanna Walter (HW) 
Sustainability Consultant at Insenti AS in Oslo, Norway, experienced in (structural) reuse processes 
• Luise Dennin (LD) 
 
LD 
What was your thesis about or how did you enter into the whole topic of circular 
 
economy in the construction business? 
 
HW 
It was about reuse of building components. We did an LCA. We tried to help a project 
 
that was going on while we were writing the thesis. That was the first full-scale reuse project 
 
in Norway. And they used concrete from a government building that was going to be torn 
 
down. So they were the first project to do that. We did some calculations to see climate 
 
emissions and the effects of reusing compared to using new materials. 
 
 
And also we wrote a lot about the requirements, because back then it was a big issue with 
 
documentation of building components. It is now as well, but at that point it was not 
 
very clear what to do. And the ones who wanted to give away reused building components, 
 
they had to take on responsibility as if they were the producer of the material. Yeah, so 
 
that was quite hard. Then the ones who wanted to give away materials had to take the risk. 
 
Because many times you don't have the documentation for all the products. 
 
 
Now it has shifted. You probably know that, but in Norway at least it has shifted so 
 
that the project that wants to reuse something has to declare what kind of qualities the 
 
product has for that specific use.  
 
LD 
So they are also in charge of testing and securing that it's safe?  
 
HW 
Yeah. 
 
LD 
So who is that it exactly? Is it the developer of the project, or is it the involved 
 
engineers? How does it work?  
 
HW 
Basically, someone has to declare that it's okay to use, and it depends on what kind of 
 
materials you want to use. If you want to do anything in structural elements, you 
 
probably have to send it, get it tested and pay for that. In that instance, it's the one who's 
 
paying for the project, the building owner, who probably will take the costs. 
 
LD 
Is there any governmental subsidies that would support these kind of projects, or is it just 
 
driven by the building owner's own intrinsic motivation believing in more sustainable 
 
construction? 
 
HW 
In Norway you have to map for possibly reusable components when you want to 
 
demolish a building or renovate a building. That's a requirement, but you don't have to 
 
do anything more than mapping. Innovation Norway has some economic support for 
==PAGE:154==
 
139 
 
projects who want to reuse components in their projects. That's one way to make it a 
 
little bit more affordable. Right now, I guess it's both. People try to use something,  in 
 
many cases, they try to avoid the constructive elements and reuse things that are a little 
 
bit easier. 
 
 
But if you want to do more full-scale reuse, then I guess you depend on some kind of 
 
funding from Innovation Norway, in many cases.  
 
LD 
What challenges did you encounter when you were involved in different reuse projects, 
 
or how did it change the way you need to approach a project?  
 
HW 
Depends on the phase, I guess. For starters, this is the way to actually be able to reuse on 
 
a bigger scale in a project. You have to be early in and make everyone know that that is a 
 
requirement and work on it from the beginning of the project. Because many times you 
 
have to make changes in the design if you want to reuse something on a bigger scale. 
 
 
But one of the hard parts, you could say, about actually doing it in the construction phase 
 
is that it probably takes more time and you can't guarantee that it's cheaper. So it takes 
 
more effort from everyone who's involved. And that makes it easy to skip it if you don't 
 
have it as a goal in the project. 
 
 
Because you have to think more about what kind of documentation do we need? Do we 
 
need any treatment of the product? It's handling in the construction phase, you have to 
 
move it around, where do you store it? All of those things make it harder to do than just 
 
buying something new. Then it's important that you have it as a goal in the project for 
 
making it happen.  
 
LD 
Is there some kind of guideline in Norway that you could stick to? Like a best practice 
 
thing? Or do you always rely on the people's experience that are involved in the project? 
 
 
HW 
Good question. I think it's more the experience of the people involved. You have some 
 
guidelines for mapping for reuse. And now you have a few reports on projects that have 
 
done this on a bigger or smaller scale. So I guess that's what you base it on, mostly.  
 
LD 
And we saw that there's this standard for reusing hollow core slabs some years now. Is 
 
there anything upcoming for a broader range of structural elements? Both concrete, 
 
steel, timber. 
 
HW 
I think I've heard that they have worked on steel. But also I think maybe I saw something 
 
about wood. Have you heard about ombyg? Ombyg is a big tent actually in Oslo. They use it 
 
as a marketplace for reuse components. 
 
 
But the people involved there and a lot of other actors, they have started this 
 
collaboration for promoting reuse. And meeting the struggles with the reuse in order to 
 
scale up in the market. I can send you a link. But I know that's a big collaboration they're 
 
working on making this easier in the industry.  
 
LD 
But who initiated this? Like private people or the government?  
 
HW 
Both private and a publicly owned company called Stadsbygd. So they were involved in 
 
this reuse of concrete slabs in the beginning. And they also initiated this tent being used as 
 
a marketplace. 
==PAGE:155==
 
140 
 
LD 
You are working as a sustainability expert. What do people request from you? What's 
 
generally missing as a common knowledge in terms of more sustainable construction?  
 
HW 
Within reuse or what do I do?  
 
LD 
In the work that you're involved in.  
 
HW 
Basically, I work as a sustainability coordinator, kind of. If you're familiar with BREEAM, 
 
I work as an accredited professional. So that's coordinating sustainability in the projects. 
 
So that could be like you're making the project, helping them with the reuse and stuff like 
 
that. But it's more like an overall consultant job. So my work is basically making 
 
everyone in the project contribute with their knowledge and making the project more 
 
sustainable.  
 
LD 
In Denmark, I feel like they already created quite a good basis to build upon. But I think 
 
the biggest problem for now is that nobody wants to take the responsibility. That's why I 
 
was asking how you deal with it in Norway. 
 
HW  
But that's the same I guess. It's hard and it depends on what kind of materials you have. 
 
What kind of standard it is and what you want to use it for. It is quite hard to make 
 
anyone guarantee for it. One thing is that this is going to be safe and okay in use. But how 
 
do you guarantee for the quality over the years? That's a hard part I guess. And then you're 
 
probably dependent on having a lot of the same kind of materials. So that you can test it 
 
in a good way. Make a statement that counts for all the materials you get from one project. 
 
 
Or if you get it from some kind of marketplace. That makes it a little bit hard in many 
 
projects. Because you probably have some amounts of the materials but not that much. 
 
So it's kind of limited. And then you have to be creative in the way you use it. If you're 
 
not searching out there on the external markets. 
 
 
Or finding reusable products somewhere else. Right now, I'm involved mostly in projects 
 
that are reusing components from an existing building. Where we are renovating. I guess 
 
it's a little bit more complex if you search out on the markets.  
 
LD 
How do people get in touch with you? How does the initial contact happen between the 
 
donors and the target builders?  
 
HW 
You have some options of marketplaces in Norway. But I guess the biggest one is Ombyg 
 
that I told you about. They are the closest we have to a bigger marketplace for the industry. 
 
But I know that many people are still using finn.no. Which is mostly for private customers 
 
and where you sell your own stuff. 
 
 
Or else you are kind of dependent on your contacts. So it's still a struggle to find that 
 
effective way to meeting the requirements and demands. So that's why I also take some 
 
initiative. 
 
 
People are really into making something happen in the project. So it's still not the 
 
standard, the go-to option unfortunately. But it's a lot more common now than when I 
 
wrote my master thesis at least but in smaller scale. 
 
==PAGE:156==
 
141 
LD 
I also believe that people are inspiring each other. Or that you can see some solutions 
 
implemented in other projects as well. 
 
HW 
So now I have some good examples of projects that have done cool stuff. So that 
 
inspires other projects as well.  
 
LD 
It is a challenging task to bring so many people together to enable reuse. 
 
HW 
And you are really dependent on that the building owner wants you to work on this and 
 
make reuse happen. Because mostly you are still testing. You can't really know the costs 
 
in the end in many cases. So there are some issues. But hopefully we will have to reuse a 
 
lot more in the coming years. 
 
LD 
Thank you for the interview. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:157==
 
142 
Interview with Lone Hedegaard Mortensen (ABC Rådgivende Ingeniører) 
20.09.2024 in Glostrup 
 
Participants 
 
• Lone Hedegaard Mortensen (LM)  
Head of Sustainability at ABC Rådgivende Ingeniører 
• Luise Dennin (LD) 
 
LD 
What has changed since you started working in the construction sector with regards to 
 
sustainability? 
 
LM 
Moving building construction in a more sustainable direction, one of the things we 
 
pointed out was that we have a look at the entire life cycle of the buildings. Actually we 
 
should do it for both cost and materials. But cost is not regulated by the building 
 
regulation. It isn’t and it cannot be part of the regulations. Because its coming from 
 
byggeloven. That’s the law behind all of the regulations. Nothing there is saying that you can 
 
actually regulate that. And maybe it's not that important because you also know that all the 
 
bygherre (edit., engl. real estate developer), they will anyways have a look at the economic 
 
factors for project. We were actually moving more towards having some demands for 
 
LCAs. So the first step was introducing the voluntary sustainability class. It came out in 
 
May 2020. Then, there was a two year test period. And you probably know that these 
 
demands that were put in place first of January 2023 with demands for LCA calculations 
 
for new buildings.  
 
(Edit: For new buildings > 1000 m2, the carbon footprint is currently limited by the Danish building 
 
regulations to 12 kgCO2eq/m2/year, in 2025 to 10, then in 2027 to 9 and by 2029 to 7.5). 
 
LD 
Yes, and they're going to be stricter in the future, right?  
 
LM 
Yes, first of July next year. There's a big change. There's more buildings affected, there's 
 
different demands for different building types. Because the current demand is 12 
 
kgCO2eq/m2/year for all building types. So some of the building types you don't really 
 
need to optimize while for others it is really hard to meet the regulations. This is to make 
 
sure that we actually optimise all our buildings. That's the reason why we suggested that it 
 
should be divided into building types. And they should have separate demands. 
 
 
And another part of the new building regulation is that we're going to include the phases 
 
A4 and A5, transport and erection of the building which concerns the construction of 
 
the building. And that's not part of the demands for the other phases. So that's like a side 
 
criteria that needs to be fulfilled. I think that's going to be quite interesting to see. 
 
LD 
Do you think it's going to impact the decision making more than it does right now? And 
 
also foster more circular construction? Because then people are actually under pressure to 
 
invent new methods of building. 
 
LM 
There's going to be two parts of it, I think. One is the development of materials in 
 
general. Just the production of materials will get less polluting or have a smaller 
 
impact just because the energy that is used is ‘greener’. It will have less impact on the 
 
environmental product declarations. So in time, that will help but it's not going to do it 
 
on its own. So we need to look into more bio-based materials and also reuse and recycle 
 
materials. 
 
==PAGE:158==
 
143 
LD 
When you're talking to engineers and architects, who do you think is most affected by it? 
 
How do engineers also have to deal with it?  
 
LM 
I think because the engineers are dealing with the load-bearing constructions, they are 
 
dealing with a lot of heavy material. And we also need to make sure that it actually stays 
 
there, right? So the load-bearing construction will have a huge share of the allowed CO2 
 
budget. I don't think that we can continue designing buildings without the construction 
 
engineers also looking into the environmental impact from their part. 
 
LD 
You're already doing that as far as I know. At least I've seen the design tool that you 
 
developed to assess your project.  
 
LM 
Yes, but that's not used on all projects. We are far from it actually. So of course we want 
 
to use it more. But when you have a building owner saying that, well, I just want it the 
 
same as I usually get it. I’ve already built this building. I want something similar. So I want 
 
the same principles in that building. 
 
 
And I think that would be more challenging. Because then we're going to say that our 
 
guess is that you won't be able to get a building permit. Because you're going to have too 
 
high CO2 emissions.  
 
LD 
But do you think there's any way on how to convince these people apart from the 
 
legislation?  
 
LM 
No. The legislation has a really high impact. The one question I get the most when I talk 
 
about how the new demands are going to be: So what happens if we don't fulfil the 
 
regulations? And you know, that's usually not a thing that's put that much effort into. I 
 
usually say that, well, you just want to make sure that that's not going to be an issue. 
 
Because we don't know exactly what will happen. We know there will be some kind of 
 
fine. And it's going to be substantial. But actually, we don't know what will happen. No, 
 
because before these climate demands, then you could have some regulations saying that 
 
if you're using too much energy, then you need to do something extra. Then you need to 
 
put up some solar panels or something like that. But that's not possible here. Because if 
 
you build some more, then you will have a bigger impact. If you put solar panels on, then 
 
you'll also emit more. So it's not really helping. So there's not this possibility as we had 
 
before to do something extra to fulfil the regulations. It's not possible anymore. So in that 
 
case, I think it's going to be a lot harder with the new demands. 
 
 
The good part is that some of the building owners, they actually already say: We want 
 
to do something that's really sustainable. Right now, we're doing some row houses. That's 
 
5 kgCO2eq/m2/year. 
 
 
And we also have some apartment buildings that are about 6.55 kgCO2eq/m2/year.  That's 
 
lower than the demands that are going to come. But it's a really good challenge for us also 
 
working with ambitious building owners. That's really helping to develop the 
 
market. And also showing that it is possible to use some reuse material. Or it is possible 
 
to use bio-based materials. Because you actually have to do a lot of stuff to get into this 
 
very low level. So I think that's really interesting. I love that some people are actually 
 
taking it serious.  
 
LD 
How do you enable such a low footprint at the end? Which elements do you focus on in 
 
the beginning? Or what does the process look like in order to optimise the design?   
==PAGE:159==
 
144 
 
LM 
Often we know what type of building we're building, right? If it's an industrial 
 
building or it's an apartment building or a single family house or something like that. We 
 
don't really do that, but anyways. Because when you know what type of building you're 
 
looking at then we have some key figures about how much things will emit. 
 
 
And that's also why we know. Because we already did this. Actually, we have looked at all 
 
the load-bearing constructions in the projects we have here at ABC, looking at how 
 
much of the budget does this uptake. Just to see. So we are also trying to have some 
 
meetings for the entire company. Trying to nurture them, saying that these are the 
 
findings we found. This is trying to make them understand how they can do some design 
 
optimisation. That will actually give a smaller environmental footprint. 
 
LD 
So you have some kind of internal database on which construction is most favourable?  
 
LM 
Actually, no. We just looked at some example projects with the foundation for example. 
 
Or this type, if it's possible to use it, this will actually help the environmental impact. If you 
 
can use a sand pillar instead, then it will help. But also with these two projects, with their 
 
ambitious building owners. We're looking into solutions, for example instead of concrete 
 
in the slabs, what can we use? Because we also have other demands. We have fire and 
 
acoustics we need to include. That gives some challenges. Because we could just pour sand 
 
on it, but the entrepreneur is not that much of a fan. Because then it will be able to go 
 
everywhere, the sand, into all cracks and if you're mounting something or you need to 
 
make holes and stuff like that, where does the sand go? So they are not that keen on that 
 
solution. 
 
 
So we are thinking about what could we do instead? How could we make a combination? 
 
We're looking at alternatives and calculating their LCA impact and are trying to show 
 
them this is the effect of doing different stuff. And in that way, try to help to make good 
 
decisions. 
 
LD 
Regarding the knowledge and tools and methods that you gained over all the years, like 
 
you said, you had different steps also in the different companies or different sites, even in 
 
the industry. What do you think would be important for the building developers, like 
 
engineers, architects, to also be educated in already at university level? What's missing, 
 
so to say?  
 
LM 
It's hard to say. Actually, it's not doing an LCA that would be the hard part. It's more 
 
figuring out how a building really works. All the things that need to be incorporated, like 
 
you need to have these thoughts about what does this mean for fire and how about the 
 
acoustics? Is it possible to build it? So it's more like the big puzzle. So I would rather 
 
have someone  that knows how a building is working than someone that's an expert in 
 
LCA calculations. Because it's easier to teach someone to do an LCA calculation than to 
 
teach them how a building works. I actually don’t think it’s important to learn a lot more 
 
about how to do an LCA calculation. Of course, you need to understand what an LCA 
 
calculation does and how it works. So I think it's very good that it's part of the education. 
 
But I wouldn't add more to that part. Because don't miss the focus that it's actually 
 
buildings that you're going to work with. So you need to have this kind of broad knowledge 
 
about buildings. That's what I think is the most important. 
 
LD 
So you would say the challenge is not so much a knowledge gap, but more on the side 
 
of the legislation.  
==PAGE:160==
 
145 
 
LM 
There is still some kind of knowledge gap, but I think it's getting better. Because the age 
 
of engineers, all the young engineers, they have much more focus on the environment. 
 
And we need to take care of that. Whereas older engineers, and this is not true for all, but 
 
more in general, maybe they have more a way of saying that, well, this is the way we used 
 
to do it. Why shouldn't that be good enough right now? Because we have always been 
 
optimising. We have always worked on doing the constructions as small as possible. But 
 
then that has been kind of merged with the entrepreneurs saying, well, I don't want to 
 
have different sizes on every floor in the building. So you also need to combine it with 
 
the possibility to actually construct the building on site – not using too many different 
 
things.  So I think we have had a time where we have had too much focus on making it 
 
easy to construct the building. 
 
 
So instead of using the minimal amount of material, we just said, okay, but if we make it 
 
this size, then we can use the same all over. And we see it right now as well. We have 
 
made some calculations for some foundations. And on-site, the entrepreneur is drilling 
 
the holes. And then he says, oh, but there are different sizes. Can I just make it the same? 
 
That would be easier for me and it will be a bit faster. And then there is actually some 
 
issues because the building owner wants the building to be done as fast as possible. The 
 
engineer wants to have it optimised so they don't use too much material. So the 
 
entrepreneur says, I can do it faster if I can make it all the same. In this case, the 
 
entrepreneur won. They did it all the same. 
 
 
This is what legislation is going to change, so what I just described is not going to 
 
happen. in that case, from my point of view, it's a waste doing the extra material. I find it 
 
hard to believe that it would take that much more time to change the drill.  
 
LD 
I mean, the same for reinforcement, right? At least from what I experienced, it's 
 
always easier to just put the same amount for the people working on site. With regards to 
 
the demounting, is it in any way incentivized to use construction methods or joints that
 
can later be easily taken apart again? Is this something that you're focusing on?  
 
LM 
Yeah. That's actually something we're trying to focus on because that's part of the entire 
 
lifecycle focus. So if we're building a building now, we also need to make sure that the 
 
building we build today actually can be dismantled when it can’t be used anymore. 
 
 
But it's really hard, I think, because how fast should you go? Because I also want to make 
 
sure that the load-bearing construction really stays there. I don't want something that will 
 
fall apart. So, actually, I think that it's okay to say that some of it cannot be taken apart, 
 
but if it's possible, then you should, of course, think about it. 
 
 
Also, we need to get away from doing internal walls with the cast-on-site concrete, 
 
because it's hard to move them around. And it's not very easy to take it down and reuse it 
 
somewhere. And I think that the LCA demands are going to change that part. 
 
 
I don't think it will be possible to have as many, at least, in the future. Maybe in some 
 
places it will make sense, because there could be some issues about fire or something, but 
 
we're not going to see it as much. We have one apartment building right now where we're 
 
using it for all internal walls. And in that case, it's also getting a DGNB certification. We 
 
told them: ‘But you cannot get these points for being able to dismantle it, and you cannot 
 
get the points for a flexible building, because it's not flexible.’. It's not flexible when you 
 
cannot move the walls around. Actually, I think the DGNB system is also affecting this, 
==PAGE:161==
 
146 
 
because it's something that's in focus there. So people are thinking, oh, there's also some 
 
points we need to gather in order to get this certification level that we need.  
 
LD 
Do you already need to show how to deconstruct or demolish the building at the end of 
 
its lifetime? Or is it planned that a plan for the end of life will be obligatory at some point?  
 
LM 
No, right now it's not obligatory in the building demands or building regulations. But it is 
 
part of the certification scheme. But it works so that you choose where you want to get 
 
the points. So you can also say, I cannot fulfil this. 
 
 
But most people would at least look at it and try to get some of the points. And actually, 
 
when we've been talking to building owners that rent buildings to companies, about how 
 
can they do it more sustainable. Because the way it works today, they would usually just 
 
take everything out in a floor and then rebuild it for a new tenant. So instead, they are 
 
actually going towards looking at what do we have and going into a dialogue with the 
 
new tenant saying, okay, so where do you need to have the walls? Can we reuse some of 
 
the doors, some of the glass walls for meeting rooms or something like that? Can we 
 
reuse some of that? And the way they are trying to do it is also to show the impact on the 
 
environmental impact. 
 
LD 
But they also save some money, right?  
 
LM 
Maybe. I wouldn't say for sure. Because it depends on if they need to move things and if 
 
they have room for storing it to restore it later. So there's actually a lot of logistics also 
 
involved. 
 
LD 
And maybe one more thing about the structural reuse and the responsibility for 
 
durability, etc. At the moment, who is responsible for the longevity of the elements or for 
 
the safety? As you're also reusing concrete elements at your project in Thoravei, is there 
 
a possibility to certify the elements in some way?  
 
LM 
Yeah, that's a part of the building regulations that there is a ‘certificeret statiker’ (edit., engl. 
 
checking engineer) So it's a construction engineer and they have some kind of certification 
 
scheme saying that depending on the consequence class, the elements are classified. So they 
 
look at who needs to certify this.  
 
LD 
And that is done by another ‘statiker’ (structural engineer) or by an official institution?  
 
LM 
No, that's a ‘statiker’. And they are a personally responsible with their signature. 
 
LD 
And is it hard to find a statiker for these kind of projects?  
 
LM 
Yes. At least for the high consequence classes.  
 
 
LD 
So who is doing that in Copenhagen? 
 
LM 
It’s the companies. We have two or three now or something like that. So it’s the 
 
construction engineers doing this certification part.  
 
LD 
So at the end also it's a personal decision, you could say, whether they sign or not, 
 
depending on the existing documentation. 
==PAGE:162==
 
147 
 
LM 
Yeah, so that can be a hard task for them, actually finding all the documentation or doing 
 
a test and stuff like that. Because they need to be really sure if they're going to put their 
 
personal signature.  
 
LD 
And because for now, as far as I know, the reused elements, they account for zero 
 
emissions in the LCA, right? In the future, do you think they're going to change this part?  
 
LM 
Yeah.  
 
LD 
How soon?  
 
LM 
It depends if they're going to do some changes so that it's going to be easier to reuse. 
 
Then you need to have like a CE marking or something that's the quality assurance. 
 
LD 
And that is done by another ‘statiker’ (structural engineer) or by an official institution?  
 
LM 
No, that's a ‘statiker’. And they are a personally responsible with their signature. 
 
LD 
And is it hard to find a statiker for these kind of projects?  
 
LM 
Yes. At least for the high consequence classes.  
 
 
 
So maybe it could have some EPD, but how do you do it without? Because right now 
 
there are companies taking materials from other industries or from buildings or whatever 
 
and doing new building products. And they have EPDs, but it's not zero because they 
 
actually do use some energy and stuff to make that new building. 
 
 
So it would be correct if they would actually add the numbers, because they do pollute. It 
 
would be better than just saying that it doesn't have any effect. Because unless it's in the 
 
same building, then you will be moving it around, and what about that transport, are you 
 
going to take that into account? So right now we're just saying, it's reused, so it has zero 
 
impact.  
 
 
I actually just remember one thing that we have become aware of, that's a challenge with 
 
the existing buildings that we are transforming or something like that, it’s actually the 
 
energy use, because new buildings have very low impact, and the energy use is also a part 
 
of the LCA demand. So actually when we’re doing calculations for those transformed 
 
buildings, actually they are not part of the demand, it's only new buildings. But what we 
 
can see is that the energy consumption takes up so much, that it actually looks like it's 
 
performing bad. 
 
 
So I actually think that's kind of a problem, because it's harder to say that, oh this is so 
 
good for the environment, because now we don't emit that much, but when you add the 
 
energy consumption, then actually it doesn't look that good.  
 
LD 
But that's due to the windows and the insulation, the way how it's constructed?  
 
LM 
It's because of the energy demand in Denmark for the energy crisis in 1973 or something 
 
like that, I think it was around there, when we had the first demand. 
 
==PAGE:163==
 
148 
 
So it's been going up a quite a lot. So the newest buildings are performing so much better 
 
than old buildings. So no matter what you do, you won't be able to be as good, 
 
without tearing the building down, which is not very nice. So I think that's kind of an 
 
issue. We didn't pay enough attention to that earlier, but I can see it is an issue now. 
 
 
We have some renovation cases right now, or transformations, where we're transforming 
 
some buildings to something new. Of course the building owner wants to know the LCA 
 
impact, and if we take into account the energy, then it actually doesn't look that good. 
 
And that's a bit annoying. I mean, it's true. It is the truth. But how do you take into 
 
account that you already did as much as is possible with this type of building? And also, 
 
I think it is in 2050, that we expect to have green power, for all of it. So how do you take 
 
that into account? 
 
LD 
But you don't take this into account right now, right? That by then everything will be 
 
green?  
 
LM 
The emission factors are going to change with the new regulations. So they're actually 
 
going to help.  
 
LD 
Is this what you mentioned before when talking about the EPDs? 
 
LM 
Yeah, but it's not the same thing. Also the energy demand in the building, within the 
 
building itself, there you will also gain something with new emission factors. And it is 
 
because we can see that electricity is getting greener, and also district heating is 
 
having this impact and stuff like that. 
 
 
So it's based on the real numbers, figures, from the energy ministry or something. 
 
Energistyrelsen (edit., The Danish Energy Agency) is – with some intervals – updating these 
 
prognoses for the emissions. The new emission factors came out last autumn, but they're 
 
not going into use until the new LCA demands on 1st of July 2025.  
 
LD 
I read through the ‘case samling’ (case collection) from Social- og Boligstyrelsen. What 
 
did you learn from the adaptive reuse and structural reuse projects with regards to the 
 
current feasibility of these projects but also regarding their environmental impact?  
 
LM 
Regarding the environmental impact, we saw that the amount of square meters is the 
 
biggest driver for how  much of an effect the reuse will affect. So if you look at the Social- 
 
og Boligstyrelsen report, you can see that there were some buildings that saved 50% of 
 
the construction impact, and for some it was about 100%. And the 50% is those that put 
 
on some extra floors or something like that. 
 
 
And the 100%, they just reused all the square meters they had already without building 
 
anything new. Because at least in Denmark, we are looking at the environmental impact 
 
of a building by its square meters. I had one of my colleagues from the project, he came 
 
to me and said, I don't understand. We did a calculation for a project. It was a parking 
 
house and we did it in steel. And then there was someone doing a concrete house, and 
 
actually you could read that what the building owner wanted  was a steel structure. 
 
 
 
But they won because they had a smaller impact. And how could that be? Because 
 
actually there shouldn't be that much of a difference, actually concrete should be a little 
 
worse. And I said, I don't know. 
 
==PAGE:164==
 
149 
 
When we were then talking to him to figure what the problem was, or what the 
 
differences were in the two projects, then he said that because it was cheaper to build the 
 
concrete parking store for each level in the house, they had another floor in the house. 
 
So they increased the area. So they had more area dividing the building on. So that was 
 
the reason they were performing better. For the same price they were able to build another 
 
floor. 
 
LD 
Which tools do you use that are integrated within the design or engineering processes to 
 
document or assess the environmental impact at ABC?  
 
LM 
We use Real-time LCA for LCA calculations and also ABConcept. And we are working 
 
together with Martin's group kind of as a quality assurance of the environmental impact 
 
numbers. Because we don't want to overestimate the performance in that early stage. We 
 
would rather go a bit conservative. 
 
LD 
I think that is it. That was a lot and very helpful, very interesting. Thank you. 
 
LM 
Don't hesitate to ask if there is something you don't get or you need to know more 
 
about. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:165==
 
150 
Interview with Anna-Olivia Myrtue (ABC Rådgivende Ingeniører) 
Structural Engineer 
27.09.2024  
 
Answers via mail 
General workflow at ABC  
Which computational design tools do you use at ABC in the structural engineering department and how do 
they improve your workflow?  
AM 
In ABC, we utilize several design tools, including Robot Structural Analysis from Autodesk and 
 
FEM Design from Strusoft for finite element modelling (FEM). Both programs have their 
 
strengths and weaknesses but are user-friendly and capable of handling complex structures. 
 
Personally, I have primarily worked with Robot, as it was the software taught during my 
 
bachelor’s degree at DTU Ballerup.  
The work of the structural engineer in reuse projects 
What will the work of structural engineers in the future consist of?  
AM 
I firmly believe that structural engineers will never become obsolete, as our work goes beyond 
 
calculations and technical knowledge. We bring a unique combination of creativity, artistic vision, 
 
and engineering expertise to the table, enabling us to create buildings and structures that take into 
 
account human inspiration all while being solidly functional. Of course, technology and 
 
automation will continue to evolve and lend us their support but they cannot match the human 
 
skill that is demanded to meet challenging issues, be creative and respond to an evolving 
 
environment both physical, social or economic.  
How are you trying to improve the carbon footprint of the structures that you are designing?  
AM 
I design for the least amount of material possible, and I always aim to optimise my calculations. I 
 
might not have much influence over the decisions like choice of material, placement of columns 
 
or beams and load distribution but I try to make best use of my role by utilizing structural 
 
elements efficiently. Less material is wasted, and less embodied carbon is saved on site in the 
 
project designed under necessary limitations, leading to a more sustainable design process.  
Do you have any structural reuse experience apart from the Thoravej project?   
AM 
No unfortunately not – Thoravej is my first experience in reusing existing structures. It is also my 
 
impression that this project is among the very first to reuse concrete elements on such a large 
 
scale.  
How did you get involved in the structural reuse project at Thoravej 29?  
AM 
My experience and interest in renovation/transformation projects were the main reasons I was 
 
originally assigned to this project. I was involved in the structural reuse project at Thoravej 29 as 
 
a construction engineer and my responsibilities included assessing the reuse of the concrete 
 
elements, ensuring their load-bearing capacity in their new function together with assessment of 
 
the surrounding existing structures and the connection between the reused elements and the 
 
existing ones.  
What were the exact tasks of ABC? Only structural design or also environmental considerations? 
==PAGE:166==
 
151 
AM 
Our responsibilities included full structural design as well as DGNB certification and project 
 
management.  
Can you describe the journey of the concrete elements from the Thoravej project that you were involved in 
as structural engineer?  
AM 
The concrete elements were not disassembled, tested, or transported off-site. The archive 
 
material was thorough, allowing the elements to be precisely identified and located on-site. As a 
 
result, the journey of the elements simply involved being disassembled, stored on-site in 
 
designated areas (to manage the significant loads), and then craned into their new positions. The 
 
disassembly process included reinforcing the elements with brackets before cutting them down. 
 
During this whole process, I contributed as an engineer by designing the reinforcements required 
 
for the disassembly and providing guidance on appropriate storage locations for the elements.  
Who carried out the testing of the concrete elements and according to which legal basis?  
AM 
The elements were not tested. 
Which standard/legislative was the basis for the calculations that you carried out for the existing concrete 
elements?  
AM 
The calculations for the existing concrete elements were based on the valid standard for concrete 
 
structures, DS/EN 1992-1-1. Additionally, the technical director from Spæncom provided 
 
guidance in selecting the appropriate material properties.  
How was the planning and structural design process different from your usual projects?  
AM 
The planning and structural design process differed primarily in that we involved the technical 
 
director of the company that originally produced the elements. He provided valuable guidance 
 
and supervision regarding the calculations.  
Which challenges occurred for you as a structural engineer during the project?  
AM 
The primary challenges were ensuring the geometry aligned correctly, cutting down the elements, 
 
and fitting new supports to accommodate the large dimensions. These issues were resolved by 
 
incorporating appropriate tolerances during the execution phase 
Who took the responsibility for reusing load-bearing concrete elements (durability, future guarantee)?  
AM 
ABC has the responsibility for the durability and load-bearing capacity of the reused concrete 
 
elements, as is typically the case for structures. The architects are responsible for the surface 
 
finish and aesthetic aspects, also in line with standard practice.  
How did you connect the reused concrete elements to the adjacent building components? (Steel elements) 
Was Design for Disassembly a priority? Why?  
AM 
I designed connections to the adjacent building components using steel brackets. These brackets 
 
ensured that the loads from the reused elements were transferred in a controlled manner, taking 
 
both the structural integrity of the reused elements and the rest of the building into account. 
 
Design for Disassembly was not a specific priority, but it is actually quite feasible due to the way 
 
the connections were designed—provided that the joint concrete can be removed in a practical 
 
way without damaging the elements.  
 
==PAGE:167==
 
152 
Which tolerances did you consider for the reused concrete elements?  
AM 
Tolerances were incorporated wherever relevant, primarily to accommodate the execution phase. 
 
This included tolerances in the geometry of the reused elements, their connections, and the 
 
adjoining building components.  
How do you ensure the fire safety? (Steel elements, any covering?)  
AM 
All steel elements were fire-protected with fire-resistant paint, and the concrete structures were 
 
verified to meet fire safety requirements.  
Did you use computational design and/or AI tools in the process and if so, how were they integrated?  
AM 
We did not use AI tools in the process. The only software we utilized was Robot Structural 
 
Analysis (FEM program), which we used to analyze vibrations and perform simple beam 
 
calculations.  
Which role does the connection design play? You mentioned it was customized? What 
research/development is necessary here in your opinion in order to scale structural reuse?  
AM 
The role of connection design is critical, as previously mentioned. Connections must ensure that 
 
both the reused elements and the components supporting them are affected in the intended way. 
 
Without proper design, we risk creating a structural system that has not been adequately analyzed, 
 
potentially leading to undesirable effects on all elements involved. The design of the connections 
 
did not rely on specific research but rather on thorough analyses to ensure the desired outcomes 
 
were achieved. In my opinion, scaling structural reuse requires a strong understanding of structural 
 
mechanics and the creativity to develop connections that are practical and tailored to the specific 
 
construction process.  
What else is in your opinion required (more general) to scale up structural reuse in Denmark?  
AM 
In my opinion, scaling up structural reuse in Denmark requires a top approach, starting with 
 
building owners and the architects who guide them in shaping their visions. It needs to be a 
 
priority and a clear ambition from the outset, as engineers typically have little influence over 
 
financial decisions or material choices. We were fortunate with the owner of the Thoravej 
 
project, who demonstrated a great deal of willingness to pursue reuse. However, the idea itself 
 
originated with the architect, and as engineers, our role was simply to make it feasible. For 
 
structural reuse to become more widespread, this kind of initiative and commitment from owners 
 
and architects is essential.  
Was there any funding for the project because of its radical reuse approach and if so from who?  
AM 
Not to my knowledge. 
Anna-Olivia Myrtue

D Primary assessment of hollow core slabs 
 
Visual assessment 
Five reclaimed HCS were provided by P. Olesen A/S and assessed after their transport to DTU. 
 
PO_1 
 
PO_1 showed mainly mechanical damage in the anchor regions. The surface is homogeneous with 
single leftovers from previous, non-structural top layers and metal connections. 
 
  
 
 
  
 
Fig. A 17 
Visual assessment PO_1 
 
==PAGE:182==
 
167 
PO_2 
 
The damage in the anchor regions includes holes protruding into the hollow cores. The surface is 
homogeneous and showed a slight red colouration. Partial concrete spalling was detected at the 
slab ends. Mortar connecting the HCS at the longitudinal sides was still attached in an irregular 
manner (Bottom right of Fig. A 18). 
 
  
  
 
  
 
Fig. A 18 
Visual assessment PO_2 
==PAGE:183==
 
168 
PO_3 
 
PO_3 also showed red coloration on the surface and presented holes from mechanical damage in 
the anchor regions. The top mortar layer is slightly irregular and leftovers from adjacent layers were 
detected. It is the only slab where mortar filling in the hollow cores was detected. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:184==
 
169 
 
 
 
Fig. A 19 
Visual assessment PO_3 
==PAGE:185==
 
170 
 
PO_4 
 
This slab has shown mechanical damage in the anchor regions, leftovers from previous, non-
structural top layers as well partial, brownish coloration on the top surface.

Visual assessment PO_4

Numerical analysis 
 
Mesh refinement analysis 
 
The mesh refinement analysis was carried out using the first model under centric loading (N = 
1500 kN). The simulation was run for 6 different mesh sizes (coarse, medium, fine). The key output 
parameter was defined as the maximum stress on the top cross section of the HCSW (See Fig. A 
27). The results were compared across the 6 simulations to identify how much they change. 
 
 
Fig. A 27 
Overview: Stress distribution at top cross section of HCSW for different mesh sizes with maximum values 
  
 
==PAGE:203==
 
188 
 
Fig. A 28 
Mesh refinement analysis: Comparison of max. v. Mises stress for different mesh sizes 
The changes of the maximum stress values after refining the maximum mesh size by 1 mm are in 
the range of ca. 0.5-6% as can be seen in Table A 3.

error. 
𝜎=OL17 ≈ 𝜎N,9 + 𝜎N,9 + 𝜎N,:

(A2) 
The true solution was calculated as 55,1 MPa. 
 
The relative error is then estimated as: 
 
𝑒F=4 = |𝜎N,9 −𝜎=OL17|
|𝜎=OL17|
 
(A3) 
 
and results as approximately 27%. 
 
Given the necessary time for the simulation of the models when employing finer meshes, a mesh 
size of 10 mm was finally chosen, which allows for a qualitative but not a precise, quantitative 
analysis as has been shown above.

==PAGE:207==
 
192 
F Testing: Setup and program 
 
 
Testing program: Step by step 
 
1. Measure the specimen (Length, width, depth, precamber). 
2. Attach the bottom and top connection to the ends of the specimen and tie them together 
with the threaded rods (M20). Ensure correct, centric positioning of the connection relative 
to the HCS. Adjustments are conducted with M24 spacers. The top connection is always 
mounted at the end which was cut by Herlevdiamanten (Smaller distance to the lifting 
anchor). 
3. The specimen is erected into a vertical position with the crane. 
4. The forklift connects to the lifting profiles at the top of the specimen. The front face 
(unreinforced) faces the forklift driver. 
5. The specimen is lifted into the machine and locks into position at the bottom connection. 
Wooden wedges allow for a more precise positioning. Hereby, the position of the HCSW 
(centric/eccentric) is measured relative to the steel cylinders. 
6. Then, the piston is moved upwards until proper contact between specimen and machine 
head is ensured, the fork lifter is still in place but can be lowered a couple of centimeters 
to release the tight contact with the U-Profiles. 
7. A sling is put around the specimen and connected to another sling that is put over the 
machine head. 
8. The position of the top of the specimen can be adjusted with a crowbar to ensure it is 
centric. 
9. Now, the forklift can be driven out. 
10. The steel rods are loosened and taken out. 
11. Then, the top connection is connected to the machine head with steel angles from both 
sides (M12 bolt, mounting from scissor lift). 
12. The temporary, horizontal M24 spacers at top and bottom connection can be taken out. 
13. The LVDTs are connected to the specimen ensuring the same is used at each level for all 
specimens. 
14. The monitor, light and camera are positioned (Position is marked on the ground). 
15. If DIC is used, check the uniform illumination of the speckled part.  
16. Take photos of the specimen from all sides and details from the connection areas. 
17. Ensure all sensors are working as intended. Start collecting data (Data logger 06). 
18. Start filming from the bridge. 
19. Now the test can start and is conducted until the failure of the element. 
20. After the test has ended, back-up data from data acquisition. 
21. Take photos of the specimen. 
22. Remove sensors. 
23. Tie the bottom and top connection together with the threaded rods. 
24. Drive the forklift in and ensure contact between top connection and the forks. 
25. Now, the piston can be lowered. 
26. Remove the wall from the testing apparatus, possibly with other slings tied around the 
specimen to prevent loose pieces from falling off.

Calculation of machine deformation (Considering 4 steel cylinders) 
Assuming a steel of class S235 on the safe side and linear-elastic material behaviour of the steel 
cylinders, their vertical deformation measures approximately 0.45 mm for a machine load of 1600 
kN corresponding to the maximum measured value during the tests): 
 
∆𝐿CA4 =
𝐹∙𝐿CA4
4 ∙𝐴CA4 ∙𝐸P
 
(A4) 
 
 
F 
Machine load: 1600 kN (max. value from the tests), divided by four cylinders 
 
ES 
Young’s Modulus: 210 GPa for S235 
 
ACyl 
The total area of a steel cylinder with d = 150 mm: 17’662 mm2 
 
LCyl 
Length of the steel cylinders: 4.2 m

H Testing protocols 
 
PO_1B_C 
Nu = 1565 kN (25.11.2024)  
 
 
 
  
Appearance of cracks and corresponding load of PO_1B_C 
 
The first visible crack, slightly skewed with respect to the load direction, was detected in the video 
at 1210 kN, while at 1237 kN a second crack suddenly appeared, accompanied by a cracking sound. 
These two initial cracks (highlighted in yellow in the pictures above) did not lead to the disturbance 
of the increasing loading (continuous graph in load-deformation diagram). No damage could be 
seen at the top of the wall indicating concrete splitting before the local excess of the concrete 
compressive strength. From ca. 1400 kN crumbling/cracking sounds could be heard resulting from 
concrete crushing at the top connection. The kink at 1429 kN in the load-deformation diagram 
results from the redistribution of the load over the damaged section. 
 
The pictures below show that at 1565 kN (ultimate load), just before failure, the second crack has 
not significantly propagated. A very sudden crack propagation of the initial crack triggered the 
failure and concrete pieces spalled of the upper part of the HCSW. As the test continued, the full-
height crack opened up further and lead to the sudden buckling of the split-off part in the 
transverse direction which broke at 1.85 m (measured from the bottom). This corresponds to the 
second kink (light rose line in the load-deformation after ultimate load).

==PAGE:227==

PO_2A_C 
Nu = 1348 kN (27.11.2024) 
 
 
 
First crack could be seen at ca. 1070 kN in the video. It propagated from underneath the middle 
flanges of the connection, diagonally towards the edge of the specimen where it stagnated. 
Crumbling sounds announced that the ultimate load of the specimen was reached at 1348 kN. The 
test continued (vertical displacement) and the concrete spalling intensified with small pieces falling 
off. The initial crack propagated and the column finally failed with the sudden crack opening over 
full-height at 1244 kN. In the load-deformation diagram it can be seen that after the ultimate load, 
the load was re-distributed (kinks) twice before the failure. 
 
 
 
 
PO_2A_C: First crack (Left), at max. load (Middle), specimen right after failure (Right) 
 
The ultimate load was reached due to the local failure at the top, while the specimen 
globally failed after the opening of a longitudinal crack over full height.

PO_2B_C 
Nu = 1409 kN (27.11.2024) 
 
 
 
Cracking sounds announced the first crack (visible at 968 kN in the video) which is located on the 
side with the lifting anchors in the middle part of the front face of the specimen. It propagated and 
suddenly opened over the height of the wall at 1040 kN (kink in the load-deformation diagram). 
The test continued: The applied load increased. Splitting sounds could be heard and the crack 
opened further. At ca. 1200 kN small concrete pieces flew off the top part. 
 
 
  
First crack (Left) and crack propagation (Right) 
 
At 1348 kN, pieces of the top corner parts of the wall started falling off, decreasing the load 
application area. A sudden bang announced the failure at 1409 kN by the longitudinal splitting of 
the concrete with multiple concrete pieces falling off from the top. The crack protruded the entire 
front and back face.

PO_4B_E20 
Nu = 1487 kN (04.12.2024) 
 
 
 
Three kinks in the graph showing the load-deformation behaviour were identified at 119 kN, 456 
kN and the biggest drop of the graph from 1011 kN to 863 kN, respectively.  
 
 
PO_4B_E20: Just before cracking (Left), right after cracking (Right) 
 
At 1011 kN, a very sudden, straight and central crack over the full specimen height at front and 
back face occurred. No mechanical damage could be heard or seen before. A crumbling and 
splitting sound started and concrete pieces started falling off from the top part of the specimen. At 
some point, the crumbling intensified, the maximum load of 1487 kN was reached and more small 
concrete pieces fell down. As can be seen from the light green graph in the diagram above, after 
reaching the ultimate load, the applied load dropped and slightly increased again before the failure 
of the specimen was triggered by the local failure of the top connection, with a sudden spalling of 
bigger concrete pieces.

Local damage at the top leading to failure 
 
 
 
Crack over full height (Front and back) 
 
After a sudden central crack over full-height, the load was redistributed and could be 
further increased. The damage of the top part with concrete pieces spalling off, reduced 
the area onto which load could be applied and ultimately lead to the failure of the 
specimen.

PO_5A_E20 
Nu = 1600 kN (05.12.2024) 
 
 
 
Smaller kinks towards reaching the ultimate load can be seen in the diagram above corresponding 
with the occurrence of the first crack and its opening (See pictures below). 
 
 
PO_5A_E20: First crack (Left and middle) and crack opening (Right) 
 
The ultimate load of the specimen was reached when suddenly, the crack propagated over full-
height which was accompanied by concrete pieces spalling off from the top of the specimen. As 
the test continued, crumbling sounds could be heard with concrete pieces falling down and the 
longitudinal crack opened. A bang signalled the local failure at the upper right part and then, the 
split-off part suddenly buckled out-of-plane (towards the front) as the load was redistributed on 
the decreased loading area.
